The Objection and Its Internal Dilemma
Ghazali presents and responds to the shamefulness objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali turns the philosophers' objection against them, showing it is an unavoidable consequence of their own positions.

Core Idea
The objection backfires: the shameful consequence is built into their own system.

Ghazali begins by stating the objection that if God only knows Himself, He would be less noble than creatures who know Him and others. He then immediately turns the tables, arguing that this very consequence is required by the philosophers' denial of divine will and the world's temporal creation. Thus they must either accept it or abandon philosophy entirely.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage from 'فإن قيل' to 'الاعتراف بأن العالم حادث بالإرادة'. Notice the structure: first the objection, then Ghazali's response.
The objection is presented as a hypothetical that leads to a shameful conclusion.
2
Identify the two options Ghazali gives
After stating that the shamefulness is necessary from the philosophers' own doctrines, Ghazali presents two alternatives: either endure it or abandon philosophy for the truth of temporal creation by will.
This is a classic dialectical move: showing that the opponent's objection undermines their own foundations.
3
Reflect on the dilemma
Consider why Ghazali phrases this as a dilemma. What does it force the philosopher to choose between?
The implication is that the philosophers cannot consistently reject the objection without giving up their core tenets.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : لو قضينا بأنه لا يعرف إلا نفسه ، لكان ذلك في غاية الشناعة ، فإن غيره يعرف نفسه ، ويعرفه ، ويعرف غيره ، فيكون في الشرف فوقه ، وكيف يكون المعلول أشرف من العلة ؟ ! قلنا : هذه الشناعة لازمة ، من مقالة الفلاسفة فى نفى الإرادة ، ونفى حدوث العالم ، فيجب ارتكابها ، كما ارتكب سائر الفلاسفة . أو لابد من ترك الفلسفة ، والاعتراف بأن العالم حادث بالإرادة . ثم يقال : بم تنكرون على من يقول من الفلاسفة : إن ذلك ليس بزيادة شرف ، فإن العلم إنما احتاج إليه غيره ، ليستفيد به كمالا ، فإنه في ذاته ناقص، والإنسان شرف بالمعقولات ، إما ليطلع على مصالحه في العواقب ، في الدنيا والآخرة ، وإما لتكمل ذاته المظلمة الناقصة ، وكذا سائر المخلوقات . وأما ذات الله سبحانه وتعالى ، فمستغنية عن التكميل ، بل لو قدر له یکمل به، لكانت ذاته ، من حيث ذاته ، ناقصة . وهذا كما قلت فى السمع والبصر ، وفى العلم بالجزئيات الداخلة تحت الزمان، فإنك وافقت سائر الفلاسفة ، على أن الله تعالى منزه عنه ، وأن المتغيرات الداخلة في الزمان ، المنقسمة إلى ما « كان » و « سيكون » لا يعرفها الأول ، لأن ذلك يوجب تغيراً في ذاته ، وتأثيراً؛ ولم يكن في سلب ذلك عنه نقصان ، بل هو كمال ، وإنما النقصان فى الحواس والحاجة إليها ، ولولا نقصان الآدمى احتاج إلى حواس ، لتحرسه عما يتعرض للتضرر به. وكذلك العلم بالحوادث الجزئية ، زعمتم أنه نقصان ، فإذا كنا نعرف الحوادث كلها ، وندرك المحسوسات كلها ، والأول لا يعرف شيئاً من الجزئيات . ولا يدرك شيئاً من المحسوسات ، ولا يكون ذلك نقصاناً ، فالعلم بالكليات العقلية أيضاً ، يجوز أن يثبت لغيره ، ولا يثبت له ، ولا يكون فيه نقصان أيضاً . وهذا لا مخرج منه .
If it is said: If we were to judge that He knows only Himself, that would be extremely shameful, for others know themselves, and know Him, and know others, so they would be above Him in nobility. How can the effect be nobler than the cause?! We say: This shamefulness is necessary from the philosophers' doctrine of negation of will and negation of the temporal origination of the world, so it must be endured as other philosophers endured it. Or it is necessary to abandon philosophy and acknowledge that the world is temporally originated by will. Then it is said: What do you deny from those philosophers who say that this is not an increase in nobility? For knowledge is only needed by others to acquire perfection, since they are deficient in themselves. And man is honored by the intelligibles either to become aware of his interests in outcomes in this world and the next, or to perfect his dark deficient essence, and likewise all creatures. But the essence of God, glorified and exalted, is independent of perfection. Rather, if it were possible for it to be perfected by something, His essence would be, in itself, deficient. And this is as you said concerning hearing and sight, and concerning knowledge of particulars that fall under time. For you have agreed with the rest of the philosophers that God, exalted, is transcendent of that, and that changing things that fall under time, divided into what 'was' and 'will be', the First does not know them, because that would necessitate change in His essence and affect; and in negating that from Him there is no deficiency, rather it is perfection. Deficiency only lies in the senses and the need for them; were it not for man's deficiency, he would need senses to guard him from exposure to harm. Likewise, knowledge of particular events: you claim it is deficiency. So if we know all events and perceive all sensible things, and the First knows nothing of particulars and perceives nothing of sensibles, and that is not a deficiency, then knowledge of universal intelligibles also may be affirmed for others and not for Him, and there is no deficiency in it either. There is no way out of this.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage is the third objection and its detailed refutation. The objection argues that if God knows only Himself, He is less noble than beings who know Him and others. The response is twofold: (1) This consequence is already entailed by the philosophers' own positions (denial of will and temporal creation), so they must accept it or abandon philosophy. (2) Knowledge is only a perfection for deficient beings; God is perfect and needs no acquisition. The analogy of sense perception is used: the philosophers agree that God does not know changing particulars because that would imply change, and they do not consider that a deficiency. Similarly, it is not a deficiency for God to lack knowledge of particulars or even universal intelligibles if it is not fitting for His transcendent essence. The argument concludes that there is no escape from this reasoning, challenging the philosophers to either accept the consistency of their own position or abandon it for the orthodox view of a world created by will.

Key Terms
شناعة
شرف
إرادة
حدوث العالم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection shows that the philosophers' God is deficient; Ghazali agrees and thus the philosophers must change their view.
Correction: Ghazali does not admit the deficiency; he argues the shame is already entailed by their own positions, so they cannot coherently object.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what do the philosophers' doctrines of no will and no temporal creation entail?
A) That God must be temporally originated.
B) That God's knowledge is limited to Himself.
C) That the objection's shamefulness is unavoidable.
D) That the world is eternal in every aspect.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali turns the objection against his opponents, showing that the alleged shame is a necessary outcome of their own principles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself objecting to something about God or His decree, pause and ask: Is my objection built on assumptions that I have not examined? What if the very thing I find shameful is actually a necessary consequence of my own limited understanding?
?
The philosophers thought they could avoid absurdity by denying God's will. In your life, have you ever avoided acknowledging a truth about God's sovereignty because its implications felt uncomfortable or shameful? What would it take for you to abandon that avoidance and accept God's will fully?
?
Ghazali says the philosophers must either endure the shameful consequence or abandon philosophy. Is there a belief or habit you cling to that leads to a spiritually shameful outcome? Are you willing to abandon it to align with truth?

Knowledge is Perfection Only for the Deficient
Ghazali argues that knowledge is a perfection only for the deficient.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that knowledge is only a perfection for deficient beings, and that God's lack of certain knowledge does not diminish His perfection.

Core Idea
Knowledge perfects the deficient; God is already perfect and needs nothing.

In this second part of the refutation, Ghazali deepens the argument. He presents the view of some philosophers that knowledge of others is not an increase in nobility for God. He explains that knowledge is needed only by those who are deficient in themselves—like humans who need to perfect their dark essence or manage their worldly and otherworldly affairs. God, being absolutely self-sufficient, has no need for such perfection, and to suppose otherwise would imply deficiency in His essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the refutation
Read from 'ثم يقال' to the end of the passage. Notice the shift from the dilemma to a positive argument about the nature of knowledge and perfection.
Ghazali now moves to a more substantive theological argument.
2
Compare human need for knowledge with God's self-sufficiency
Identify Ghazali's claim that humans and other creatures need knowledge to perfect themselves or to benefit in outcomes. Then contrast with God's essence, which is independent of perfection.
The key contrast is between need (iḥtiyāj) and self-sufficiency (istighnā).
3
Analyze the analogy with sense perception
Ghazali uses the example of hearing, sight, and knowledge of particulars: philosophers already agree that God is free from these because they'd imply change. Yet they do not consider this a deficiency. Why then should knowledge of universals be any different?
This is a powerful consistency argument.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Read the final sentence: 'There is no way out of this.' Consider why Ghazali believes the argument is unanswerable for the philosophers.
The argument forces the philosophers to either accept the conclusion or abandon their position on divine knowledge.

Source Text

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's lack of knowledge of particulars (and universals) is a real deficiency because knowledge is always a perfection.
Correction: Ghazali argues that knowledge is only a perfection for those who are deficient; for a perfect being, lacking certain knowledge is not a defect but consistent with transcendence.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why is knowledge necessary for humans but not for God?
A) Because humans are ignorant by nature.
B) Because humans need to perfect their deficient essence, whereas God is self-sufficient.
C) Because God is too great to know small things.
D) Because knowledge is a burden for God.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge is a perfection for deficient beings; God's freedom from needing knowledge is a sign of His absolute perfection, not a flaw.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself seeking knowledge to feel whole, as if without it you are less? How does Ghazali's argument that knowledge perfects the deficient confront your own motives for learning? Will you now examine whether your pursuit of knowledge stems from a sense of lack rather than genuine curiosity?
?
If God's perfection lies in His independence from needing knowledge, then what does that say about your own attachment to your intellect? Are you willing to see your greatest intellectual achievements as a sign of your need rather than your worth? How would it change your daily life to truly believe that your worth is not in what you know but in your connection to the Self-Sufficient?
?
Ghazali argues that even admitting God could be perfected by something would imply deficiency. Have you ever worshiped a God you secretly thought needed your worship, or felt that your devotion adds to His glory? How does this passage correct that, and what would it mean for you to worship a God who is utterly independent of you?

Refutation of the Claim that God's Lack of Particular Knowledge is No Deficiency
Ghazali refutes the philosophers' claim that God's lack of knowledge of particulars is not a deficiency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that God's lack of knowledge of particulars is not a deficiency, and that the philosophers' position leads to inconsistency.

Core Idea
Knowledge is for the deficient; God's independence means lacking it is perfection, not deficiency.

In this passage, Ghazali confronts a philosophical objection: if God does not know particulars, that might seem like a deficiency. He turns the tables by arguing that knowledge is sought by imperfect beings for perfection. Since God is absolutely self-sufficient, lacking such knowledge is not a deficiency but a mark of perfection. He then pushes the argument to its logical conclusion, showing the philosophers are forced into inconsistency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation. Familiarise yourself with the flow and key terms.
Notice the rhetorical structure: Ghazali presents a hypothetical objection and then refutes it.
2
Identify the core argument
Identify Ghazali's main point: knowledge is needed only by deficient beings; God is self-sufficient, so lacking it is not a deficiency.
Look for the phrase 'إنما احتاج إليه غيره'.
3
Examine the analogy with senses
See how Ghazali uses the example of hearing and sight to parallel knowledge of particulars. The philosophers already agree God lacks senses, and that's perfection.
This strengthens his argument by showing inconsistency if they reject it for knowledge.
4
Understand the final twist
Ghazali extends the logic: if lacking particulars is no deficiency, then lacking universals could also be no deficiency, which the philosophers would deny.
This reduces their position to absurdity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حدوث العالم ، فيجب ارتكابها ، كما ارتكب سائر الفلاسفة . أو لابد من ترك الفلسفة ، والاعتراف بأن العالم حادث بالإرادة
ثم يقال : بم تنكرون على من يقول من الفلاسفة : إن ذلك ليس بزيادة شرف ، فإن العلم إنما احتاج إليه غيره ، ليستفيد به كمالا ، فإنه في ذاته ناقص، والإنسان شرف بالمعقولات ، إما ليطلع على مصالحه في العواقب ، في الدنيا والآخرة ، وإما لتكمل ذاته المظلمة الناقصة ، وكذا سائر المخلوقات .
وأما ذات الله سبحانه وتعالى ، فمستغنية عن التكميل ، بل لو قدر له یکمل به، لكانت ذاته ، من حيث ذاته ، ناقصة . وهذا كما قلت فى السمع والبصر ، وفى العلم بالجزئيات الداخلة تحت الزمان، فإنك وافقت سائر الفلاسفة ، على أن الله تعالى منزه عنه ، وأن المتغيرات الداخلة في الزمان ، المنقسمة إلى ما « كان » و « سيكون » لا يعرفها الأول ، لأن ذلك يوجب تغيراً في ذاته ، وتأثيراً ؛ ولم يكن في سلب ذلك عنه نقصان ، بل هو كمال ، وإنما النقصان فى الحواس والحاجة إليها ، ولولا نقصان الآدمى احتاج إلى حواس ، لتحرسه عما يتعرض للتضرر (١) به . وكذلك العلم بالحوادث الجزئية ، زعمتم أنه نقصان ، فإذا كنا نعرف الحوادث كلها ، وندرك المحسوسات كلها ، والأول لا يعرف شيئاً من الجزئيات . ولا يدرك شيئاً من المحسوسات ، ولا يكون ذلك نقصاناً ، فالعلم بالكليات العقلية أيضاً ، يجوز أن يثبت لغيره ، ولا يثبت له ، ولا يكون فيه نقصان أيضاً
وهذا لا مخرج منه .
The occurrence of the world, so they must commit to it, as the rest of the philosophers committed. Or they must abandon philosophy and admit that the world is originated by will. Then it is said: On what basis do you reject what some philosophers say: that this is not an increase of honor, for knowledge is only needed by other than Him to gain perfection through it, for it is in itself deficient; and man is honored by intelligibles, either to learn about his interests in the outcomes, in this world and the Hereafter, or to perfect his dark deficient essence, and likewise the rest of creation. As for the Essence of God, exalted and high, it is independent of perfection; rather, if it were possible for Him to be perfected by it, His Essence would, in itself, be deficient. And this is as I said regarding hearing and sight, and regarding knowledge of particulars that fall under time, for you agreed with the rest of the philosophers that God is transcendent above that, and that changing things within time, divided into what 'was' and 'will be', the First does not know them, because that would necessitate change in His Essence and influence; and there was no deficiency in depriving Him of that, rather it is perfection. Deficiency is only in the senses and the need for them; and were it not for man's deficiency, he would need senses to protect him from what he might be harmed by. Likewise, knowledge of particular events, you claim it is deficiency. So if we know all events and perceive all sensibles, and the First does not know any particulars nor perceive any sensibles, and that is not deficiency, then knowledge of universal intelligibles also may be affirmed for other than Him and not be affirmed for Him, and there is also no deficiency in that. There is no escape from this.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a philosophical objection. Ghazali begins by referencing the earlier argument about the world's origination, forcing the philosophers to either accept the full conclusion or abandon philosophy. He then presents a hypothetical question from a philosopher who claims that God's lack of knowledge of particulars is not a deficiency. Ghazali argues that knowledge is sought by deficient beings for perfection, but God is self-sufficient; if He were perfected by knowledge, He would be deficient. He then draws an analogy with the senses: just as the philosophers agree that God is free from senses like hearing and sight (since those imply change), and that this removal is perfection, so too with knowledge of temporal particulars. If the philosophers claim that knowledge of particulars is deficiency, then God's lack of it is not deficiency. Ghazali then extends the argument: if God's lack of knowledge of particulars and sensibles is no deficiency, then likewise lack of knowledge of universal intelligibles could be no deficiency. He concludes that this argument leaves no escape for the philosophers.

Key Terms
العلم بالجزئيات
الكليات العقلية
السمع والبصر
النقصان
التكميل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Ghazali is arguing that God lacks knowledge of universals as well.
Correction: Ghazali does not claim God lacks knowledge of universals; rather, he shows that if the philosophers' logic is followed, they would have to accept that, which they do not, thus revealing inconsistency.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why is it not a deficiency for God to lack knowledge of particulars?
A) Because God is perfect and knowledge is only needed by deficient beings.
B) Because He is too great to care about particulars.
C) Because knowledge of particulars is impossible for any being.
D) Because the philosophers proved it.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali demonstrates that the philosophers' claim that God's lack of particular knowledge is no deficiency leads to the absurd conclusion that God could also lack knowledge of universals without deficiency, which they would deny, exposing their inconsistency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever measure your worth by what you know? How does Ghazali's argument that knowledge is for the deficient challenge your own assumptions about pride in intellect?
?
If God's lack of certain knowledge is perfection, what does that say about your own constant need to know? Can you accept uncertainty in your life as a reflection of divine perfection?
?
The philosophers' inconsistency was exposed because they applied a standard to God that they would not apply to themselves. Where in your own life do you apply double standards—judging others by rules you don't follow?

Philosophers Cannot Prove God's Self-Knowledge
Ghazali contrasts Muslim and philosopher methods for proving God's self-knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that the philosophers' denial of divine will and action prevents them from proving that God knows Himself.

Core Idea
Without will and action, God becomes as unconscious as fire.

Ghazali continues his critique of the philosophers by tackling their claim that God knows Himself. He shows that their own principles—denying will and action—actually undermine any proof for self-knowledge. In this passage, he contrasts the sound Muslim method with the philosophers' dead-end reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once. Notice the structure: the Muslim method, the philosophers' position, the objection, and Ghazali's retort.
The text ends mid-sentence; focus on the completed argument.
2
Identify the Muslim inference chain
Trace how Muslims move from the world's origination by will to will, knowledge, life, and finally self-knowledge. This is presented as a sound method.
This chain connects action to self-awareness.
3
Examine the philosophers' analogy
Ghazali compares God to fire and the sun—natural forces that produce effects without self-consciousness. Understand why this analogy is devastating for the philosophers.
If God is like fire, He cannot know Himself.
4
Understand the objection and retort
The objection says not knowing oneself implies death. Ghazali turns it around: if God lacks will, power, hearing, etc., why must He have self-knowledge?
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
5
Reflect on Ghazali's strategy
Note that Ghazali uses the philosophers' own premises to force a contradiction. He is not endorsing their view; he is showing its consequences.
The common misunderstanding is that Ghazali agrees with them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة [ ۱۲ ]
في تعجيزهم
عن إقامة الدليل على أنه يعرف ذاته أيضاً
فنقول : المسلمون لما عرفوا حدوث العالم بإرادته ، استدلوا بالإرادة على العلم، ثم بالإرادة والعلم جميعاً ، على الحياة ، ثم بالحياة على أن كل حي، يشعر بنفسه، وهو حى ، فيعرف أيضاً ذاته ، فكان هذا منهجاً معقولا ، في غاية المتانة

Issue [12]
In incapacitating them from establishing proof that He knows His Essence as well.
We say: When the Muslims knew the origination of the world by His will, they inferred from will to knowledge, then from will and knowledge together to life, then from life that every living being is conscious of itself, and He is living, so He also knows His Essence. This was a reasonable method, extremely sound.
As for you, when you denied will and bringing into existence, and claimed that what emanates from Him emanates by necessity and nature, then what intentionality is there in that His Essence is an essence from which only the first effect exists? Then the second effect follows from the first effect, until the complete order of existents, but despite that it is not conscious of itself, like fire from which heat follows, and the sun from which light follows, and neither of them knows its own essence, just as it does not know other than itself. Rather, whoever knows his essence knows what emanates from him, so he knows other than himself. And we have shown from their doctrine that He does not know other than Him, and we have compelled those who disagree with them to agree with them by the rule of their position; and if He does not know other than Him, it is not improbable that He does not know Himself.
If it is said: Everyone who does not know himself is dead, so how can the First be dead? 
We say: That then follows from the course of your doctrine, for there is no distinction between you and one who says: Everyone who does not act with will, power, and choice, and does not hear or see, is dead; and whoever does not know other than him is dead. For if it is permissible that the First be devoid of all these attributes, what need does He have to know His Essence? Then if they return to...
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage is a polemical argument (mas'ala) in which Ghazali aims to show that the philosophers are unable to prove that God knows Himself, given their own principles. He first presents the Muslim method: from the world's origination by will, they infer will, then from will and action they infer life, and from life they infer self-knowledge, since every living being is conscious of itself. This is a sound rational method. Then he contrasts the philosophers' position: they deny will and action (إحداث) and claim that the world emanates by necessary natural necessity. He asks what intentionality there is in such a view, and compares God to fire or the sun, which produce effects without self-consciousness. He argues that if God does not know other than Himself (as previously established from their doctrine), then it is plausible that He does not know Himself. An objection is raised: not knowing oneself implies death, so how can the First be dead? Ghazali answers by turning the objection back on them: according to their own doctrine, they have already stripped God of will, power, choice, hearing, sight — so why stop at self-knowledge? If He can lack all those without being dead, why must He have self-knowledge? The passage ends with 'then if they return to...' suggesting a further counter-argument that is cut off.

Correction: Ghazali is using a reductio ad absurdum: if you accept the philosophers' premises, you cannot prove God's self-knowledge, and you end up with a God less conscious than fire.

The Muslim Method of Proving God's Self-Knowledge
Ghazali outlines the Muslim theologians' inferential chain from world's temporality to God's self-knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sequential inferential argument used by Muslim theologians to prove that God knows His own essence.

Core Idea
God's self-knowledge is proven through a chain: will, knowledge, life, self-awareness.

Imam al-Ghazali presents the method of the Muslim theologians (mutakallimun) for establishing that God knows Himself. This argument is not based on speculation but on a rational inference from the attributes of will, knowledge, and life that are already established from the temporality of the world. Follow the logical steps closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic text and its literal translation. Notice the concise structure of the argument.
2
Identify the starting point
The argument begins with the temporality of the world (ḥudūth al-ʿālam), known through God's will. This is the foundation.
The temporality of the world is a key doctrine in kalām.
3
Trace the inference chain
Follow each step: from will to knowledge, from will and knowledge to life, from life to self-awareness. Understand why each step follows.
Will implies knowledge because one cannot will something without knowing it. Life is necessary for will and knowledge to exist. Any living being is aware of itself.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Since God is living, He knows His own essence. Ghazali endorses this as a rational and solid method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسلمون لما عرفوا حدوث العالم بإرادته استدلوا بالإرادة على العلم ثم بالإرادة والعلم جميعاً على الحياة ثم بالحياة على أن كل حي يشعر بنفسه وهو حي فيعرف أيضاً ذاته فكان هذا منهجاً معقولا في غاية المتانة
The Muslims, when they knew the temporality of the world by His will, they inferred from will to knowledge, then from will and knowledge together to life, then from life that every living thing is aware of itself, and He is living, so He also knows His essence. So this was a rational method of utmost solidity.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage defines the Muslim theologians' method for proving that God knows His own essence. It is a sequential argument: first, they establish that the world is temporally originated (hadith) through God's will. From will, they infer knowledge, because will requires knowledge of what is willed. Then from will and knowledge together, they infer life, because these are attributes of a living being. Then from life, they infer self-awareness, because every living being is aware of itself. Since God is living, He knows Himself. Ghazali endorses this as a rational and solid method.

Key Terms
حدوث العالم
إرادة
علم
حياة
شعور بالذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the argument is circular because it assumes self-awareness to prove self-knowledge.
Correction: The argument is not circular: it uses the universal principle that every living being is aware of itself, not the specific conclusion, to deduce God's self-knowledge from His life.

Checkpoint
According to the Muslim theologians' argument, what is the immediate inference from the attribute of life?
A) That God is knowing
B) That every living being is aware of itself
C) That the world is temporally originated
D) That will implies knowledge

Key Takeaway
The Muslim theologians prove God's self-knowledge through a rational chain: from the world's temporality (via will) to knowledge, life, and finally self-awareness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God knows every detail of your being—your thoughts, intentions, and hidden sins—why do you still find it easy to disobey Him as if He were unaware?
?
You have learned that God's will, knowledge, life, and self-awareness are proven through a chain. But does your daily life reflect that you truly believe God is aware of you, or do you act as if you are unseen?
?
The proof shows that every living being is conscious of itself. Are you conscious of God's presence as He is conscious of you? What would change if you lived with that constant awareness?

The Philosophers' Difficulty in Proving Self-Knowledge
Ghazali argues that philosophers cannot prove God's self-knowledge due to denial of will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that the philosophers cannot prove God's self-knowledge due to their denial of will.

Core Idea
Without will, self-knowledge is impossible.

Ghazali confronts the philosophers' claim that God knows Himself. He shows that if the universe emanates necessarily from God, like heat from fire, then God could be unconscious, like fire. This passage exposes the flaw in the philosophers' view.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let Ghazali's own argument come through before you interpret.
Notice the rhetorical question in the middle.
2
Understand the analogy
Ghazali compares the First to fire and the sun, which produce effects necessarily but are not conscious. Reflect on why this analogy undermines self-knowledge.
3
See how Ghazali uses their own principles
Ghazali notes that he has already shown from their school that God does not know other things. Using that, he concludes it is not far that God does not know Himself. Trace the logical chain.
This is a powerful argumentative move.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما أنتم فإذا نفيتم الإرادة والإحداث وزعمتم أن ما يصدر عنه يصدر بلزوم على سبيل الضرورة والطبع فأي تعمد في أن تكون ذاته ذاتاً من شأنها أن يوجد منها المعلول الأول فقط؟ ثم يلزم من المعلول الأول المعلول الثاني إلى تمام ترتيب الموجودات ولكنه مع ذلك لا يشعر بذاته كالنار يلزم منها السخونة والشمس يلزم منها النور ولا يعرف واحد منهما ذاته كما لا يعرف غيره بل من يعرف ذاته يعرف ما يصدر عنه فيعرف غيره وقد بينا من مذهبهم أنه لا يعرف غيره وألزمنا من خالفهم من ذلك موافقتهم بحكم وضعهم وإذا لم يعرف غيره لم يبعد ألا يعرف نفسه
As for you, when you deny will and bringing-into-existence and claim that what emanates from Him emanates by necessity and nature, then what deliberateness is there that His essence be an essence from which only the first effect proceeds? Then from the first effect follows the second effect until the complete arrangement of existents, but nevertheless it is not conscious of itself, like fire from which heat proceeds and the sun from which light proceeds, and neither of them knows itself just as it does not know other. Rather, whoever knows himself knows what emanates from him, so he knows other. And we have shown from their school that He does not know other, and we have compelled those who disagree with them to agree with them by virtue of their own position. And if He does not know other, it is not far that He does not know Himself.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage presents the core problem for the philosophers who deny divine will and attribute the world's emanation to necessary natural causation. If the First is like fire or the sun—causing effects necessarily without awareness—then there is no reason to think He is conscious of Himself. Ghazali notes that on their principles, the First does not know other things (as argued elsewhere), and if He does not know others, it is plausible that He does not know Himself either. Thus the philosophers cannot prove divine self-knowledge.

Misconception: The philosophers explicitly deny that God knows Himself.
Correction: Ghazali argues that they cannot prove it given their own principles, not that they deny it outright.

A) Because they deny will and say all emanation is necessary
B) Because they believe God is ignorant
C) Because they use the Muslim method
D) Because they prove God's knowledge of others first

Key Takeaway
On the philosophers' own premises, God's self-knowledge cannot be proven because necessary emanation does not require self-consciousness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Ghazali uses the example of fire and sun to show that necessary emanation lacks self-awareness. Reflect: Do you ever treat God like a natural law—expecting blessings without turning to Him in willful submission? What would change if you truly believed He has will and knowledge?
?
The philosophers' premises lead to a dead end. Are there areas in your faith where you rely on human reasoning more than revelation? What is one belief you hold about God that you cannot prove but accept by faith?

Objection and Reply: Self-Knowledge and the Charge of Death
An objection that lacking self-knowledge entails death, and Ghazali's reply using the philosophers' own principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses a dialectical argument to show that the philosophers' denial of divine attributes leads to absurdities, using the charge of death as an example.

Core Idea
Ghazali turns the objection back on the philosophers by exposing their own inconsistency.

This brief exchange captures a classic dialectical move: the objector tries to force a contradiction on Ghazali's view that God does not know Himself. Instead of defending that view, Ghazali points out that the philosophers have already stripped God of will, power, and sense-perception—so by their own logic, they cannot object to lacking self-knowledge. The lesson shows how a skilled debater uses his opponent's premises.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic once, then the translation. Notice the structure: an objection followed by a sharp reply.
2
Understand the objector's argument
The objector says 'whoever does not know himself is dead.' Apply that to God: if God does not know Himself, He would be dead. The objector thinks this is impossible.
3
See how Ghazali turns the tables
Ghazali responds by listing attributes the philosophers already deny to God (will, power, hearing, sight). He says: by your own criteria, you already call God 'dead' for lacking those. So why is self-knowledge special?
This is not Ghazali's own view; he is using the philosophers' standards against them.
4
Reflect on the dialectical method
Ask yourself: Is Ghazali really saying God is dead? No. He is showing that the objection is inconsistent with the philosophers' other commitments.
This passage is a good example of reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: كل من لا يعرف نفسه فهو ميت فكيف يكون الأول ميتاً؟! قلنا: فقد لزمكم ذلك على مساق مذهبكم إذ لا فصل بينكم وبين من قال: كل من لا يفعل بإرادة وقدرة واختيار ولا يسمع ولا يبصر فهو ميت ومن لا يعرف غيره فهو ميت فإن جاز أن يكون الأول خالياً هذه الصفات كلها فأي حاجة به إلى أن يعرف ذاته؟!
If it is said: Whoever does not know himself is dead, so how can the First be dead? We say: Then that follows upon you according to your doctrine, for there is no distinction between you and one who says: Whoever does not act by will, power, and choice, and does not hear or see, is dead, and whoever does not know other than himself is dead. If it is permissible that the First be devoid of all these attributes, then what need does He have to know His essence?!
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage records an objection and Ghazali's reply. The objector argues that if God does not know Himself, He would be dead, which is impossible. Ghazali turns the argument against the philosophers: on their own principles, they already deny God attributes like will, power, hearing, and sight, and they also deny that He knows other things. By their own criteria, they would have to call God 'dead' based on that. If they allow God to be devoid of those attributes, then why should He be required to have self-knowledge? The objection thus fails to refute them, but rather exposes their inconsistency.

إرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some readers think Ghazali is affirming that God is dead or that self-knowledge is necessary for life.
Correction: Ghazali is not making a positive claim. He is using the philosophers' own criteria to show their objection is inconsistent. The passage is dialectical, not doctrinal.

D) He denies the premise that not knowing oneself is death

Key Takeaway
Ghazali uses the philosophers' own criteria to show that their objection about self-knowledge is inconsistent with their other denials of divine attributes.

?

Objection: Immaterial Implies Self-Intellection, and Ghazali's Reply
An objection and Ghazali's dismissal as arbitrary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali rejects the philosophers' claim that immaterial things are self-intellecting as arbitrary and unproven.

Core Idea
Immateriality does not automatically prove self-intellection without a valid proof.

In this passage, Ghazali addresses a further objection from the philosophers: that anything immaterial is inherently an intellect and thus intellects itself. He dismisses this as mere arbitrary assertion (tahakkum) without any demonstrative proof (burhan). Understanding this refutation is key to seeing how Ghazali demands rigorous proof in theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic sentence and its translation carefully. Notice that this is a complete objection-and-reply in one sentence.
2
Identify the two sides
The philosophers' claim: immaterial = self-intellecting. Ghazali's response: arbitrary, no proof. Ask yourself why Ghazali considers it arbitrary.
3
Reflect on the method
Consider that in kalam, assertions about God's attributes require proof. Ghazali is upholding this standard here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن عادوا إلى أن كل ما هو بريء عن المادة فهو عقل بذاته فيعقل نفسه فقد بينا أن ذلك تحكم لا برهان عليه
If they return to the claim that everything free from matter is an intellect by itself and consequently intellects itself, then we have shown that this is arbitrary, with no proof for it.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This short passage presents a further objection and its refutation. The philosophers might argue that whatever is immaterial is inherently an intellect, and thus automatically intellects itself. Ghazali dismisses this as mere arbitrary assertion (tahakkum) without demonstration (burhan). In kalam methodology, claims require proof, especially concerning the divine essence. So this objection does not stand.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Immateriality alone proves that something must intellect itself.
Correction: Ghazali teaches that this inference is not logically necessary; it must be proven, and the philosophers have provided no proof. Immateriality does not automatically entail self-intellection.

Checkpoint
What is Ghazali's critique of the claim that immaterial things are self-intellecting?

B) He says it is arbitrary and lacks proof
C) He says it applies only to God
D) He says it is a rational necessity

Key Takeaway
Ghazali insists that claims about divine attributes must be supported by proof; the bare assertion that immaterial entails self-intellection is arbitrary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Objection from Living/Dead Division and Final Refutation
Ghazali refutes the objection from living/dead division using the analogy of sight and the principle of nobility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's refutation of the philosophers' argument from the division of existents into living and dead, and his use of analogy to show the flaw in their reasoning.

Core Idea
Effect can be nobler than cause – Ghazali turns their own reasoning against them.

This passage presents the final objection from the philosophers, who argue from the division of existents into living and dead that the First must be living and self-conscious. Ghazali responds by questioning why an effect cannot be nobler than its cause, and uses an analogy from sight and blindness to expose the inconsistency in their method. He ultimately shows that their argument fails to prove God's self-knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the philosophers' claim: existent divides into living and dead; living is nobler, so the First is living and self-conscious. Note how they present it as a proof.
2
Ghazali's initial challenge
Ghazali calls the claim arbitrary. He asks: why can't an effect be nobler than its cause? This is not self-evident.
Notice that Ghazali doesn't reject the premise outright but questions the logical connection.
3
The sight analogy
Ghazali invents a parallel argument: if someone said 'existent divides into seeing and blind; seeing is nobler, so the First is seeing', the philosophers would reject it, saying nobility is in independence. Apply the same to self-knowledge.
This reveals their inconsistency: they apply a standard only when it suits them.
4
Conclusion and implications
Ghazali concludes that the philosophers are forced to deny God's self-knowledge on their own principles, since no evidence apart from temporal creation (which they reject) points to it.
This ends the discussion of divine attributes in this context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: البرهان عليه أن الموجود ينقسم: إلى حي وإلى ميت والحي أقدم وأشرف من الميت والأول أقدم وأشرف فيكون حيا وكل حي يشعر بذاته إذ يستحيل أن يكون في معلولاته الحي وهو لا يكون حيا. قلنا: هذه تحكمات فإنا نقول: لم يستحيل أن يلزم ممن لا يعرف نفسه من يعرف نفسه بالوسائط الكثيرة أو بغير واسطة؟! فإن كان المحيل لذلك كون المعلول أشرف من العلة فلم يستحيل أن يكون المعلول أشرف من العلة؟! وليس هذا بدهيا؛ ثم لم تنكرون على من يقول: إن شرفه في أن وجود الكل غيره ربما عرف أشياء سوى لا في علمه؟! والدليل عليه أن يرى ويسمع ذاته وهو لا يرى ولا يسمع؛ ولو قال قائل: الموجود ينقسم: إلى البصير والأعمى والعالم والجاهل فليكن البصير أقدم وليكن الأول بصيراً وعالماً بالأشياء لكنتم تنكرون ذلك وتقولون: ليس الشرف في البصر والعلم بالأشياء بل في الاستغناء عن البصر والعلم وكون الذات بحيث يوجد منه الكل الذي فيه العلماء وذو الأبصار. فكذلك لا شرف في معرفة الذات بل في كونه مبدأ لذوات المعرفة وهذا شرف مخصوص به فبالضرورة يضطرون إلى نفي علمه أيضاً بذاته إذ لا يدل عليه شيء سوى حدوث العالم وبفساد ذلك سوى الإرادة ولا يدل على الإرادة من ذلك يفسد هذا كله على من يأخذ هذه الأمور من نظر العقل فجميع
If it is said: The proof for it is that existent divides into living and dead, and the living is more prior and nobler than the dead, and the First is most prior and noblest, so He is living. And every living being is conscious of itself, for it is impossible that among His effects there be a living being while He Himself is not living. We say: These are arbitrary claims. For we say: Why is it impossible that from one who does not know himself, there should follow one who knows himself, whether through many intermediaries or without intermediary? If the prohibitive factor is that the effect is nobler than the cause, then why is it impossible that the effect be nobler than the cause? This is not self-evident. Then why do you not deny one who says: His nobility lies in that the existence of everything other than Him perhaps knows other things while He is not in His knowledge? And the evidence for it is that He perceives and hears His essence while He does not see or hear. And if someone were to say: Existent divides into the seeing and the blind, the knowing and the ignorant; then let the seeing be prior and let the First be seeing and knowing of things, you would deny that and say: Nobility is not in sight and knowledge of things, but in independence from sight and knowledge, and in that the essence is such that from it proceeds the whole that contains knowers and seers. Likewise, there is no nobility in knowledge of the self, but rather in His being the principle of knowing essences, and this is a nobility particular to Him. So necessarily they are forced to deny His knowledge of His essence as well, since nothing indicates it except the temporality of the world, and with the corruption of that there is only will, and will is not indicated by that—all this is corrupted for one who takes these matters from intellectual reflection.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage contains the final objection and Ghazali's extended refutation. The objector argues from the division of existents into living and dead: since the living is more noble, and the First is the most noble, He must be living; and every living being is conscious of itself. Ghazali responds by calling these arbitrary claims. He challenges the impossibility of a cause that does not know itself producing an effect that does know itself, even through intermediaries. He questions why an effect cannot be nobler than its cause, which is not self-evident. He then uses an analogy: if someone argued from the division into seeing and blind that the First must be seeing, the philosophers themselves would reject that, saying that His nobility lies in independence from such attributes, not in possessing them. Similarly, self-knowledge is not necessarily noble; rather, nobility is in being the source of all knowing beings. Thus, the objector's argument does not force the conclusion. Ghazali concludes that the philosophers are necessarily forced to deny God's knowledge of His essence, because there is no evidence for it except the temporality of the world (which they deny) and will (which they also deny). On their own terms, they cannot prove it.

Misconception: One might think Ghazali is saying God has no attributes.
Correction: Rather, he is refuting the philosophers' specific argumentative strategy; Ghazali affirms divine attributes elsewhere.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Ghazali refutes the philosophers' argument from nobility by showing that effect can be nobler than cause, and by using analogy to expose inconsistency, ultimately forcing them to deny God's self-knowledge on their own principles.

?
If God's true nobility lies in being independent of attributes you might value—like knowledge or sight—does that confront your personal conception of greatness? Will you let that challenge reshape how you worship and relate to Him?

Ghazali's Refutation of the Philosophers' Proof for Divine Self-Knowledge
Ghazali refutes the philosophers' proof for divine self-knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's refutation of the philosophers' proof that God knows Himself, and why he considers their arguments arbitrary conjectures rather than certain knowledge.

Core Idea
Ghazali shows that the philosophers' proof for divine self-knowledge is based on arbitrary assumptions, not reason.

The philosophers argued that because God is immaterial, He must be an intellect that knows Himself. In this passage, Ghazali dismantles that argument, exposing its logical flaws and questioning the very notion that self-knowledge is a necessary attribute of the First. He then offers an alternative understanding of divine nobility and concludes that all such rational proofs are mere conjectures.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' claim and Ghazali's dismissal
Read the first part of the passage where Ghazali states the philosophers' claim and immediately dismisses it as arbitrary. Notice his directness.
2
Analyze the philosophers' syllogism
Examine the syllogism they present: existence divides into living and dead, the living is nobler, so the First is living and thus knows Himself. Then read Ghazali's rebuttal questioning why an effect cannot be nobler than its cause.
Ghazali calls this a 'tahakkum' (arbitrary statement).
3
Explore Ghazali's alternative view of nobility
Read the section where Ghazali suggests that true nobility is not in self-knowledge but in being the source of knowing essences. Compare this with the philosophers' view.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider Ghazali's final point that all the philosophers' attributes are based on conjectures and that reason is bewildered concerning divine attributes.
Note his critique of their self-admiration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كل ما هو برىء عن المادة ، فهو عقل بذاته ، فيعقل نفسه ، فقد بينا أن ذلك تحكم ، لا برهان عليه
فإن قيل : البرهان عليه ، أن الموجود ينقسم : إلى حى ، وإلى ميت ، والحى أقدم وأشرف من الميت والأول أقدم وأشرف ، فيكون حيا ، وكل حى يشعر بذاته ، إذ يستحيل أن يكون في معلولاته الحى ، وهو لا يكون حيا .
قلنا : هذه تحكمات ، فإنا نقول : لم يستحيل أن يلزم ، ممن لا يعرف نفسه ، من يعرف نفسه ، بالوسائط الكثيرة ، أو بغير واسطة ؟ ! فإن كان المحيل لذلك ، كون المعلول أشرف من العلة ، فلم يستحيل أن يكون المعلول أشرف من العلة ؟ ! وليس هذا بدهياً :
ثم لم تنكرون على من يقول : إن شرفه ، في أن وجود الكل غيره ربما عرف أشياء سوى لا في علمه ؟ ! والدليل عليه ، و يرى ويسمع ذاته ، وهو لا يري ، ولا يسمع ؛ ولو قال قائل : الموجود ينقسم : إلى البصير ، والأعمى ؛ والعالم ، والجاهل ؛ فليكن البصير أقدم ، وليكن الأول بصيراً ، وعالماً بالأشياء ؛ لكنتم تنكرون ذلك ، وتقولون : ليس الشرف في البصر والعلم بالأشياء ، بل فى الاستغناء عن البصر والعلم ، وكون الذات بحيث يوجد منه الكل، الذي فيه العلماء وذو الأبصار .
فكذلك لا شرف في معرفة الذات ، بل في كونه مبدأ ، لذوات المعرفة وهذا شرف مخصوص به
فبالضرورة يضطرون ، إلى نفي علمه أيضاً بذاته ، إذ لا يدل عليه شيء ، سوى حدوث العالم ، وبفساد ذلك سوي الإرادة ، ولا يدل على الإرادة من ذلك يفسد هذا كله ، على من يأخذ هذه الأمور من نظر العقل ، فجميع ما ذكروه من صفات الأول ، أو نفوه ، لا حجة لهم عليه ، إلا تخمينات وظنون ، يستنكف الفقهاء منها في الظنيات
ولا غرو ، لو حار العقل فى الصفات الإلهية ، ولا عجب ، إنما العجب من إعجابهم ، بأنفسهم وبأدلتهم ، ومن اعتقادهم أنهم عرفوا هذه الأمور ، معرفة يقينية ، مع ما فيها من الخبط والخبال .
Everything that is free from matter is an intellect by itself, so it intellects itself. We have shown that this is arbitrary, not a proof. If it is said: The proof for it is that existence is divided into living and dead, and the living is prior and nobler than the dead, and the First is prior and nobler, so He is living; and every living thing is aware of itself, since it is impossible that among its effects there should be the living while it is not living. We say: These are arbitrary statements. For we say: Why is it impossible that from one who does not know himself there should follow one who knows himself, through many intermediaries or without intermediary? If the preventing factor is that the effect is nobler than the cause, then why is it impossible that the effect be nobler than the cause? This is not self-evident. Then why do you not reject the one who says: His nobility lies in that the existence of the whole other than Him perhaps knows things other than [Himself] not in His knowledge? The proof for it is that He sees and hears His essence, while He does not see nor hear. If someone said: Existence is divided into the seeing and the blind, and the knowing and the ignorant; let the seeing be prior, and let the First be seeing and knowing things; you would reject that and say: Nobility is not in sight and knowledge of things, but in being independent of sight and knowledge, and in the essence being such that from it arises the whole which contains the knowers and those possessing sight. Likewise, there is no nobility in knowledge of the self, but in His being a source of knowing essences, and this is a nobility particular to Him. They are forced necessarily to deny His knowledge of Himself as well, since nothing indicates it except the origination of the world, and with the corruption of that, [it indicates] only will, and does not indicate will from that. All this is corrupted for one who takes these matters from rational consideration. So all that they have mentioned of the attributes of the First, or denied, there is no proof for them except conjectures and opinions, from which even jurists shrink in matters of probability. And no wonder if reason is bewildered concerning the divine attributes, and it is not astonishing. The astonishing thing is their self-admiration and admiration of their proofs, and their belief that they have known these matters with certain knowledge, despite the confusion and nonsense in them.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage is a refutation. Ghazali begins by stating the philosophers' claim: everything free from matter is an intellect and intellects itself. He immediately dismisses this as arbitrary, without proof. He then presents their syllogism: existence is divided into living and dead, the living is prior and nobler, so the First is living; every living thing is aware of itself, and it is impossible that a living effect comes from a non-living cause. Ghazali responds by calling these arbitrary. He questions why it is impossible for an effect to be nobler than its cause, stating that this is not self-evident. He then turns the tables: if someone claimed the First's nobility lies in being independent of knowledge and sight, rather than having them, the philosophers would reject that. So similarly, they should not claim nobility in self-knowledge. He argues that the First's true nobility is in being the source of knowing essences, not in knowing Himself. He concludes that the philosophers are forced to deny even His self-knowledge, because nothing indicates it except the world's createdness, which indicates will, not knowledge. All their supposed proofs are mere conjectures, and their confidence is misplaced.

Correction: Ghazali does not deny divine self-knowledge; he only refutes the philosophers' rational proof for it, showing that their method leads to absurdities and that such matters are beyond reason.

Ghazali's Critique of Philosophers on Divine Attributes
Ghazali dismisses philosophers' arguments on divine attributes as conjectures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's critique of the philosophers' arguments on divine attributes as lacking certain knowledge and being based on conjectures.

Core Idea
Ghazali accuses philosophers of basing divine attributes on mere guesses, not proofs.

In this passage, Ghazali delivers a sharp critique of the philosophers' approach to discussing God's attributes. He asserts that their affirmations and negations lack genuine proof and are built on shaky conjectures. This lesson explores his dismissal and the contrast he draws between their self-assurance and the actual weakness of their arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to understand Ghazali's tone and claims. Notice his use of strong words like 'conjectures' and 'opinions'.
2
Identify the critique
Identify what Ghazali says the philosophers lack and what they rely on instead. He states they have no proof, only conjectures and opinions.
Note the contrast with the jurists who would be ashamed of such weak reasoning.
3
Reflect on Ghazali's conclusion
Reflect on why Ghazali emphasizes the philosophers' self-admiration and false claim to certain knowledge. Consider the implication for theological methodology.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما ذكروه من صفات الأول ، أو نفوه ، لا حجة لهم عليه ، إلا تخمينات وظنون ، يستنكف الفقهاء منها في الظنيات
ولا غرو ، لو حار العقل فى الصفات الإلهية ، ولا عجب ، إنما العجب من إعجابهم ، بأنفسهم وبأدلتهم ، ومن اعتقادهم أنهم عرفوا هذه الأمور ، معرفة يقينية ، مع ما فيها من الخبط والخبال .
What they have mentioned of the attributes of the First, or what they have negated, they have no proof for it except conjectures and opinions, from which the jurists would be ashamed even in matters of opinion. And it is no wonder if the intellect becomes perplexed concerning the divine attributes, and it is not astonishing; the only wonder is at their self-admiration and their proofs, and at their belief that they have known these matters with certain knowledge, despite the confusion and nonsense they contain.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage is a general critique by Ghazali of the philosophers' arguments regarding the attributes of God (the First). He states that whatever they affirm or negate of the attributes, they have no real proof, only conjectures (takhminat) and opinions (zunun). He emphasizes that even jurists (fuqaha') would be ashamed to rely on such weak reasoning in matters of probability (zanniyyat). Then he comments that it is not surprising if the intellect becomes bewildered when faced with divine attributes; what is truly surprising is the philosophers' arrogance in their own arguments and their conviction that they have attained certain knowledge (ma'rifa yaqiniyya) when in reality their discourse is full of confusion (khabt) and nonsense (khabal).

God's Knowledge of Particulars: The Eclipse Example
Ghazali introduces the problem of God's knowledge of particulars and illustrates with an eclipse example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that if God knew changing particulars, His knowledge would change, using the example of a solar eclipse.

Core Idea
If God's knowledge changed with time, His essence would change.

Ghazali begins his refutation of the philosophers' claim that God knows particulars in a universal way by first explaining their position. He then provides a concrete example: a solar eclipse with its three temporal states. This example helps us see why the philosophers' model of 'universal knowledge' does not avoid the problem of change in God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Notice the structure: first the problem statement, then the explanation of the philosophers' positions, then the eclipse example.
The passage ends mid-sentence; the argument continues but is cut off here.
2
Identify the two philosophical positions
Ghazali mentions two groups: those who say God knows only Himself, and those (like Ibn Sina) who say God knows other things with a universal knowledge that does not change over time.
The second view is the target of the refutation.
3
Understand the three states of the eclipse
The eclipse has three temporal states: future (will occur), present (occurring), and past (has occurred). These correspond to three different pieces of knowledge for a human knower.
List them: will be, is, was.
4
Connect the states to human knowledge
For each state, humans have a distinct knowledge. These knowledges are multiple and different. Their succession in the knower's mind would necessitate change in the knowing essence.
This is the key point: change in knowledge implies change in the knower.
5
Apply the argument to God
If God knew the eclipse in the same way humans do, His knowledge would change with time, implying a change in His essence—which is impossible for God.
The passage cuts off before stating the conclusion explicitly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة ( ١٣ ] في إبطال قولهم : إن الله - تعالى عن قولهم - يعلم الجزئيات المنقسمة بانقسام الزمان : إلى الكائن ، وما كان ، وما يكون .
وقد اتفقوا على ذلك ، فإن من ذهب منهم إلى أنه لا يعلم إلا نفسه ، فلا يخفى هذا مذهبه ، ومن ذهب إلى أنه يعلم غيره – وهو الذي اختاره ابن سينا » - فقد زعم أنه يعلم الأشياء ، علماً كليا ، لا يدخل تحت الزمان ؛ ومع ذلك زعم أنه لا يعزب عن علمه ، ولا يختلف بالماضى والمستقبل والآن ؛ مثقال ذرة في السموات ولا في الأرض ، إلا أنه يعلم الجزئيات بنوع كلى . ولا بد أولا من فهم مذهبهم ، ثم الاشتغال بالاعتراض .

ونبين هذا بمثال ، وهو أن الشمس مثلا ، تنكسف بعد أن لم تكن منكسفة ، ثم تنجلى ، فتحصل لها ثلاثة أحوال – أعنى الكسوف – :
حال هو فيها معدوم ، منتظر الوجود ، أي سيكون
وحال هو فيها موجود ، ، أي هو كائن .
.
وحال ثالثة هو فيها معدوم ، ولكنه كان من قبل
ولنا بإزاء هذه الأحوال الثلاثة ، ثلاثة علوم مختلفة
فإنا نعلم أولا : أن الكسوف معدوم ، وسيكون .
وثانياً : أنه كائن .
.
وثالثاً : أنه كان ، وليس كائناً الآن .
وهذه العلوم الثلاثة متعددة ومختلفة ، وتعاقبها على المحل يوجب تغير الذات العالمة ، فإنه لو علم بعد الانجلاء ، أن الكسوف موجود الآن ، كما كان قبل ،
Problem (13): On refuting their statement that God—exalted is He above their saying—knows particulars divided by the division of time: into what is, what was, and what will be. And they have agreed upon that, for whoever of them holds that He knows only Himself, then this school of thought is not hidden, and whoever holds that He knows other than Himself—and this is the one chosen by Ibn Sina—he has claimed that He knows things with a universal knowledge that does not enter under time; and with that he claimed that not even the weight of a dust particle in the heavens or the earth escapes His knowledge, and it does not differ with past, future, and present; except that He knows particulars in a universal manner. And first we must understand their doctrine, then engage in objection. And we will clarify this with an example, which is that the sun, for example, eclipses after it was not eclipsed, then clears, so there occur for it three states—I mean of the eclipse: A state in which it is non-existent, awaiting existence, i.e., it will be. And a state in which it is existent, i.e., it is. And a third state in which it is non-existent, but it was before. And corresponding to these three states, we have three different knowledges: For we know first: that the eclipse is non-existent and will be. And second: that it is existent. And third: that it was, and is not existent now. And these three knowledges are multiple and different, and their succession upon the locus necessitates change of the knowing essence, for if after the clearing he knew that the eclipse is existent now, as it was before,
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of Ghazali's refutation (ibtaal) of the philosophers' claim that God knows particulars in a universal way. He first states the problem: God does not know changing particulars over time in a way that would imply change in His knowledge. He then outlines the two philosophical positions: those who say God only knows Himself, and those (like Ibn Sina) who say God knows other things but with a universal knowledge that does not change over time. Ghazali says we must first understand their doctrine before objecting. He then provides an example of a solar eclipse. The eclipse has three temporal states: future (will occur), present (occurring), and past (has occurred). Corresponding to these three states, a human knower has three different pieces of knowledge: knowledge that it will happen, knowledge that it is happening, and knowledge that it happened. These three knowledges are distinct, and their succession in the knower's mind would necessitate a change in the knowing essence. Ghazali then hints that if God were to know the eclipse in the same way, He would have to have changing knowledge, which is impossible for God. The passage ends mid-sentence, indicating the argument continues.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Ghazali denies that God knows particulars at all.
Correction: Rather, he argues against the philosophers' specific model of universal knowledge that avoids change, not that God has no knowledge of particulars.

C) Because the eclipse is not a real event
D) Because the philosophers deny the existence of time

Key Takeaway
The eclipse example demonstrates that if God's knowledge changed with temporal events, His essence would change, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that God knows every detail of your life without His essence changing, how would that change the way you view your past mistakes and future plans?
?
The eclipse example shows that human knowledge changes with time. How does the fact that God's knowledge never changes affect your trust in His decrees?
?
Think of a time you doubted that God was aware of a specific struggle. After learning that God's knowledge is unchanging and perfect, what specific event or situation in your life do you need to surrender to His eternal knowledge?

God's Knowledge of Particulars: The Eclipse Example
Ghazali presents the philosophers' view on God's knowledge of particulars with the eclipse example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' claim that God knows particulars in a universal manner, and Ghazali's use of the eclipse example to expose the problem of change in knowledge.

Core Idea
God's knowledge cannot change because He is unchanging, yet particulars change—a dilemma.

In this passage, Ghazali introduces the philosophers' (especially Ibn Sina's) position on God's knowledge of temporal particulars. He uses the example of a solar eclipse to illustrate a fundamental problem: if God's knowledge is eternal and unchanging, how can it encompass events that have past, present, and future states? The passage sets up the key argument that knowledge follows the known, so any change in the known would require a change in the knower—impossible for God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the three states of the eclipse and the three knowledges.
Notice how Ghazali first presents the philosophers' view, then gives the example.
2
Identify the three states and corresponding knowledges
Find in the passage where Ghazali describes the three states of the eclipse (future, present, past) and the three knowledges that correspond to them. Understand why these knowledges are distinct.
3
Understand the philosophers' dilemma
Ask yourself: if God's knowledge is unchanging, how can He know the eclipse in three different states? The philosophers claim He knows it universally. Think about whether this solves the problem.
This connects to the core idea of the lesson.
4
Reflect on the alternative account
Notice how Ibn Sina explains God's knowledge of the eclipse through its causes and the celestial motions. Consider if this avoids the problem of change.

Issue (13): On invalidating their claim that God – exalted above their claim – does not know the particulars divided by the division of time: into the present, what was, and what will be. And they have agreed on that. For whoever among them holds that He knows only Himself, this is not hidden from his doctrine. And whoever holds that He knows other than Himself – and this is what Ibn Sina chose – he claims that He knows things with a universal knowledge that does not enter under time. Yet he claims that not a speck's weight in the heavens or the earth escapes His knowledge, except that He knows particulars in a universal manner. It is necessary first to understand their doctrine, then to occupy [ourselves] with the objection. And we explain this with an example: namely, the sun, for example, becomes eclipsed after not having been eclipsed, then clears. So for it – meaning the eclipse – three states occur: a state in which it is non-existent, awaiting existence, i.e. it will be; a state in which it is existent, i.e. it is; and a third state in which it is non-existent, but it was before. And corresponding to these three states, we have three different knowledges. For we know first: that the eclipse is non-existent and will be. Secondly: that it is existent. Thirdly: that it was, and is not now. And these three knowledges are multiple and different, and their succession upon the subject necessitates change in the knowing essence. For if, after clearing, he knew that the eclipse is now existent as it was before, that would be ignorance, not knowledge. And if at its existence he knew that it is non-existent, he would be ignorant. So some of these do not stand in place of others. They claimed that God, exalted, His state does not differ in these three states, for that would lead to change. And as long as His state does not differ, it is inconceivable that He know these three matters. For knowledge follows the known. So if the known changes, knowledge changes; and if knowledge changes, then the knower necessarily changes, and change is impossible for God, exalted. Yet with this, he claimed that He knows the eclipse and all its attributes and accidents, but with a knowledge He is described by in pre-eternity and perpetuity, and it does not differ. For example, He knows that the sun is existent, and that the moon is existent, for they both proceed from Him by the mediation of the angels, which they, by their terminology, call separate intellects. And He knows that they move in periodic motions, and that between their two spheres there is an intersection at two points, which are the head and the tail, and that they meet in some states at the two nodes, so the sun is eclipsed – i.e., the body of the moon interposes between it and the eyes of the beholders, so the sun is hidden from the eyes – and that when it passes the node by a certain amount, for example, which is a year, then it is eclipsed again, and that eclipse is in its entirety, or a third, or a half, and that it lasts an hour or two, and so on, up to the states of the eclipse and its accidents. And nothing of His knowledge escapes, but His knowledge of this, before the eclipse, at the time of the eclipse, and after clearing, is on the same pattern, does not differ, and does not necessitate change in His essence. And likewise His knowledge of all events, for they only occur by causes, and those causes have other causes, until they terminate in the celestial periodic motion. And the cause of the periodic motion is the heavens themselves, and the cause of the souls' movement is the desire to resemble God, exalted, and the near angels. So all is known to Him, i.e., it is disclosed to Him with a single proportionate disclosure, upon which time does not affect. And with this, the state of the eclipse – it is not said: that He knows that the eclipse...
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage is a presentation of the philosophers' (specifically Ibn Sina's) claim about God's knowledge of particulars, followed by Ghazali's illustration of the problem it entails. The passage begins by stating the topic: refuting the philosophers' claim that God does not know particulars subject to temporal division (past, present, future). It notes that some philosophers say God knows only Himself, while Ibn Sina claims He knows things with a universal knowledge not subject to time, yet nothing escapes Him. Ghazali then says we must first understand their doctrine before objecting. He gives the example of a solar eclipse, which has three temporal states: future (it will be), present (it is), and past (it was). For humans, these three states correspond to three distinct acts of knowledge. If someone knew the eclipse as present after it had cleared, that would be ignorance. The three knowledges cannot replace each other because the object changes. The philosophers, however, claim that God's state does not differ in these three states, because change is impossible for Him. They assert that knowledge follows the known, so if the known changes, knowledge changes, and the knower changes, which is impossible for God. Yet they claim that God knows the eclipse and all its attributes and accidents with an eternal, unchanging knowledge. Ghazali then elaborates the philosophers' alternative account: God knows the sun and moon as existent, their motions, the intersection of their spheres, the conditions causing eclipses, etc., all in a timeless manner. He knows events through their causal chain ending in the celestial periodic motion, which is caused by the souls' desire to resemble God. Thus all is known to God in a single, proportionate disclosure unaffected by time. The passage ends abruptly, likely introducing an objection that is cut off.

Misconception: Some might think the philosophers denied God's knowledge of particulars entirely.
Correction: Ibn Sina affirms God's knowledge of particulars, but in a universal mode that is unchanging and timeless.

C) Because the eclipse is not a real event.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali uses the eclipse to show that an unchanging God cannot have knowledge that changes with temporal particulars, despite the philosophers' attempt to explain it through universal knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe that God knows every detail of your life—every thought, every action, every failure. Yet do you live as though His knowledge is complete and unchanging, or do you sometimes feel you can 'surprise' Him with your repentance or choices? ?
The philosophers argued that God's knowledge is not affected by time—He sees your past, present, and future as a single, present reality. If that is true, then your past sins are not hidden from Him, nor are they forgotten. How does this confront your desire to hide or minimize your wrongs? Will you now, in light of His all-encompassing knowledge, bring them to Him in genuine repentance, or will you continue to live as if some parts of you are unknown?
?
