The Core Argument: God's Knowledge Cannot Change
Ghazali presents the philosophers' argument against change in God's knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' syllogism that if God's knowledge changed, He would change, which is impossible.

Core Idea
Change in knowledge implies change in the knower; therefore God's knowledge is unchanging.

The philosophers begin with a critical premise: God's state does not differ across temporal states, because any difference would imply change. Since change is impossible for God, His knowledge of events before, during, and after must be identical. This lesson breaks down their logical chain.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation, focusing on the logical flow from premise to conclusion.
Notice the three temporal states mentioned: before, during, after.
2
Identify the premise
The philosophers state that God's state does not differ in the three states, because that would lead to change.
3
Trace the consequences
They argue that if the known changes, knowledge changes, and if knowledge changes, the knower changes. This would be impossible for God.
This is a key principle: knowledge follows the known.
4
Understand the conclusion
Therefore, God's knowledge must be unchanging and eternal, covering all events in a single act.
The conclusion sets up the example of the eclipse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فزعموا أن الله تعالى، لا تختلف حاله، في هذه الأحوال الثلاثة، فإنه يؤدي إلى التغير، وما لم تختلف حاله، لم يتصور أن يعلم هذه الأمور الثلاثة، فإن العلم يتبع المعلوم، فإذا تغير المعلوم، تغير العلم، وإذا تغير العلم، فقد تغير العالم لا محالة، والتغير على الله تعالى محال
ومع هذا جميع أنه يعلم الكسوف صفاته وعوارضه، ولكن علماً هو متصف به في الأزل والأبد، ولا يختلف، مثل أن يعلم مثلا، أن الشمس موجودة، وأن القمر موجود، فإنهما حصلا منه، بوساطة الملائكة، التي سموها باصطلاحهم، عقولاً مجردة؛ ويعلم أنها تتحرك، حركات دورية، ويعلم أن بين فلكيهما تقاطعاً، على نقطتين هما الرأس والذنب، وأنهما يجتمعان في بعض الأحوال، في العقدتين، فتنكشف الشمس، أي يحول جرم القمر، بينها وبين أعين الناظرين، فتستتر الشمس عن الأعين، وأنه إذا جاوز العقدة مثلا، بمقدار كذا، وهو سنة مثلا، فإنها تنكسف مرة أخرى، وأن ذلك الانكساف يكون في جميعها، أو ثلثها، أو نصفها، وأنه يمكث ساعة أو ساعتين، وهكذا إلى أحوال الكسوف وعوارضه؛ ولا يعزب عن شيء، ولكن علمه بهذا، قبل الكسوف، وفي حال الكسوف، وبعد الانجلاء، على وتيرة واحدة، لا يختلف، ولا يوجب تغيراً في ذاته
وكذا علمه بجميع الحوادث، فإنها إنما تحدث بأسباب، وتلك الأسباب لها أسباب أخر، إلى أن تنتهي إلى الحركة الدورية، السماوية. وسبب الحركة الدورية نفس السموات، وسبب تحريك النفوس، الشوق إلى التشبه بالله تعالى، والملائكة المقربين
فالكل معلوم له، أي هو منكشف له، انكشافاً واحداً متناسباً، لا يؤثر فيه الزمان. ومع هذا، فحال الكسوف، لا يقال: إنه يعلم أن الكسوف موجود الآن، ولا يعلم بعده أنه انجلى الآن.
وكل ما يجب في معرفته الإضافة إلى الزمان، فلا يتصور أن يعلمه، لأنه يوجب التغير
They claimed that God, exalted, does not have differing states in these three states [before, during, after], because that would lead to change, and if His state did not differ, it is inconceivable that He knows these three things, for knowledge follows the known, so if the known changes, knowledge changes, and if knowledge changes, then the knower necessarily changes, and change is impossible for God. And with all this, He knows the eclipse, its attributes and accidents, but with a knowledge that He is described with in eternity and everlasting, and it does not differ, such that He knows, for example, that the sun exists and that the moon exists, for they both emanate from Him through the mediation of the angels, which they in their terminology call abstract intellects; and He knows that they move in circular motions, and He knows that between their two spheres there is an intersection at two points, which are the head and tail [nodes], and that they meet in some states at the two nodes, so the sun is eclipsed, i.e., the moon's body interposes between it and the eyes of observers, so the sun is hidden from eyes, and that when it passes the node by a certain amount, which is a year for example, it eclipses again, and that the eclipse is of all of it, or a third, or half, and that it lasts an hour or two, and so on regarding the states of the eclipse and its accidents; and nothing escapes [Him], but His knowledge of this, before the eclipse, during the eclipse, and after the clearance, is on one mode, it does not differ, and does not necessitate change in His essence. Likewise His knowledge of all events, for they only occur through causes, and those causes have other causes, until they terminate at the circular motion of the heavens. And the cause of circular motion is the heavens themselves, and the cause of the souls' movement is the desire to resemble God, exalted, and the close angels. So all is known to Him, i.e., unveiled to Him, in a single proportionate unveiling, time does not affect it. And with this, regarding the state of the eclipse, it is not said that He knows that the eclipse exists now, and He does not know after it that it has cleared now. And everything the knowledge of which requires temporal addition, it is inconceivable that He knows it, because it necessitates change.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage is a presentation and implicit refutation of the philosophers' doctrine regarding God's knowledge of temporal particulars. It begins by stating their premise: God's state does not differ across the three temporal states (before, during, after) because difference would imply change, and change is impossible for God. Since knowledge follows the known, if the known changes, knowledge changes; that would mean God changes, which is impossible. Therefore, they argue, God's knowledge must be unchanging and cover all temporal events in a single, eternal intuition. The example of an eclipse is given: God knows all details of the eclipse—its causes, its attributes, its timing—but with one unchanging knowledge that does not vary before, during, or after the event. This knowledge is not temporally indexed; God does not know that the eclipse exists now versus that it has cleared now, because such knowledge would involve temporal addition and thus change. They trace all events ultimately to the circular motion of the heavens, caused by the souls' desire to resemble God. All is known to God in a single unveiling unaffected by time. The core claim is that God's knowledge is timeless and universal, not particular in a temporal sense.

تغير
علم
معلوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's knowledge changes over time like human knowledge.
Correction: The philosophers argue that change in God is impossible, so His knowledge must be eternal and unchanging, covering all events in a single timeless act.

Checkpoint

A) Knowledge creates the known.
B) Knowledge follows the known.
C) Knowledge is independent of the known.
D) The known follows knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' argument hinges on the principle that knowledge follows the known, so any change in the known would necessitate change in God's essence.

The Eclipse Example: One Unchanging Knowledge
Ghazali presents the eclipse example illustrating the philosophers' doctrine.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers apply their doctrine of unchanging divine knowledge to the specific example of a lunar or solar eclipse.

Core Idea
God knows all details of an eclipse with a single eternal knowledge, unaffected by time.

The philosophers illustrate their claim with the example of an eclipse. They assert that God knows the sun, moon, their motions, nodes, and every eclipse occurrence—before, during, and after—with one unchanging knowledge. This lesson unpacks how they reconcile temporal events with an eternal knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the example
Read the passage describing God's knowledge of the eclipse and its details.
Notice the many specific details listed.
2
Identify the components of knowledge
God knows the sun and moon as emanations, their circular motions, the nodes, and the conditions of the eclipse (extent, duration, etc.).
3
Understand the temporal scope
God's knowledge covers the eclipse before it happens, during it, and after it clears – all with the same knowledge.
The text says: 'قبل الكسوف، وفي حال الكسوف، وبعد الانجلاء، على وتيرة واحدة'
4
Appreciate the conclusion
This knowledge does not necessitate any change in God's essence.
The example is meant to support the earlier syllogism.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فزعموا أن الله تعالى، لا تختلف حاله، في هذه الأحوال الثلاثة، فإنه يؤدي إلى التغير، وما لم تختلف حاله، لم يتصور أن يعلم هذه الأمور الثلاثة، فإن العلم يتبع المعلوم، فإذا تغير المعلوم، تغير العلم، وإذا تغير العلم، فقد تغير العالم لا محالة، والتغير على الله تعالى محال
ومع هذا جميع أنه يعلم الكسوف صفاته وعوارضه، ولكن علماً هو متصف به في الأزل والأبد، ولا يختلف، مثل أن يعلم مثلا، أن الشمس موجودة، وأن القمر موجود، فإنهما حصلا منه، بوساطة الملائكة، التي سموها باصطلاحهم، عقولاً مجردة؛ ويعلم أنها تتحرك، حركات دورية، ويعلم أن بين فلكيهما تقاطعاً، على نقطتين هما الرأس والذنب، وأنهما يجتمعان في بعض الأحوال، في العقدتين، فتنكشف الشمس، أي يحول جرم القمر، بينها وبين أعين الناظرين، فتستتر الشمس عن الأعين، وأنه إذا جاوز العقدة مثلا، بمقدار كذا، وهو سنة مثلا، فإنها تنكسف مرة أخرى، وأن ذلك الانكساف يكون في جميعها، أو ثلثها، أو نصفها، وأنه يمكث ساعة أو ساعتين، وهكذا إلى أحوال الكسوف وعوارضه؛ ولا يعزب عن شيء، ولكن علمه بهذا، قبل الكسوف، وفي حال الكسوف، وبعد الانجلاء، على وتيرة واحدة، لا يختلف، ولا يوجب تغيراً في ذاته
وكذا علمه بجميع الحوادث، فإنها إنما تحدث بأسباب، وتلك الأسباب لها أسباب أخر، إلى أن تنتهي إلى الحركة الدورية، السماوية. وسبب الحركة الدورية نفس السموات، وسبب تحريك النفوس، الشوق إلى التشبه بالله تعالى، والملائكة المقربين
فالكل معلوم له، أي هو منكشف له، انكشافاً واحداً متناسباً، لا يؤثر فيه الزمان. ومع هذا، فحال الكسوف، لا يقال: إنه يعلم أن الكسوف موجود الآن، ولا يعلم بعده أنه انجلى الآن.
وكل ما يجب في معرفته الإضافة إلى الزمان، فلا يتصور أن يعلمه، لأنه يوجب التغير
They claimed that God, exalted, does not have differing states in these three states [before, during, after], because that would lead to change, and if His state did not differ, it is inconceivable that He knows these three things, for knowledge follows the known, so if the known changes, knowledge changes, and if knowledge changes, then the knower necessarily changes, and change is impossible for God. And with all this, He knows the eclipse, its attributes and accidents, but with a knowledge that He is described with in eternity and everlasting, and it does not differ, such that He knows, for example, that the sun exists and that the moon exists, for they both emanate from Him through the mediation of the angels, which they in their terminology call abstract intellects; and He knows that they move in circular motions, and He knows that between their two spheres there is an intersection at two points, which are the head and tail [nodes], and that they meet in some states at the two nodes, so the sun is eclipsed, i.e., the moon's body interposes between it and the eyes of observers, so the sun is hidden from eyes, and that when it passes the node by a certain amount, which is a year for example, it eclipses again, and that the eclipse is of all of it, or a third, or half, and that it lasts an hour or two, and so on regarding the states of the eclipse and its accidents; and nothing escapes [Him], but His knowledge of this, before the eclipse, during the eclipse, and after the clearance, is on one mode, it does not differ, and does not necessitate change in His essence. Likewise His knowledge of all events, for they only occur through causes, and those causes have other causes, until they terminate at the circular motion of the heavens. And the cause of circular motion is the heavens themselves, and the cause of the souls' movement is the desire to resemble God, exalted, and the close angels. So all is known to Him, i.e., unveiled to Him, in a single proportionate unveiling, time does not affect it. And with this, regarding the state of the eclipse, it is not said that He knows that the eclipse exists now, and He does not know after it that it has cleared now. And everything the knowledge of which requires temporal addition, it is inconceivable that He knows it, because it necessitates change.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage is a presentation and implicit refutation of the philosophers' doctrine regarding God's knowledge of temporal particulars. It begins by stating their premise: God's state does not differ across the three temporal states (before, during, after) because difference would imply change, and change is impossible for God. Since knowledge follows the known, if the known changes, knowledge changes; that would mean God changes, which is impossible. Therefore, they argue, God's knowledge must be unchanging and cover all temporal events in a single, eternal intuition. The example of an eclipse is given: God knows all details of the eclipse—its causes, its attributes, its timing—but with one unchanging knowledge that does not vary before, during, or after the event. This knowledge is not temporally indexed; God does not know that the eclipse exists now versus that it has cleared now, because such knowledge would involve temporal addition and thus change. They trace all events ultimately to the circular motion of the heavens, caused by the souls' desire to resemble God. All is known to God in a single unveiling unaffected by time. The core claim is that God's knowledge is timeless and universal, not particular in a temporal sense.

Key Terms
كسوف
عقول مجردة
حركة دورية
رأس
ذنب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, how does God know the eclipse?
A) With a new knowledge at the time of the eclipse.
B) With one unchanging knowledge that is the same before, during, and after.
C) By observing the eclipse as it happens.
D) Through the angels who report it.

Key Takeaway
The eclipse example demonstrates how an omniscient God can know temporal events in their full detail without any change in His eternal knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God knew every detail of your past, present, and future before you were born, yet you still act as if you can surprise Him? How does this change the way you approach your sins and your repentance?
?
The philosophers claim God's knowledge does not change. If so, then when you turn to Him in dua, He already knew your prayer before you made it. How does this affect the sincerity and urgency of your supplication?
?
When you reflect on your life's timeline—your past regrets and future anxieties—do you view them as separate moments or as part of a single divine knowing? And if God's knowledge is one and unchanging, can you trust Him completely with every moment?

Causal Chain and Timeless Knowledge of All Events
Ghazali presents the philosophers' causal argument for timeless knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers extend the eclipse model to all events via a causal chain, and why knowledge requiring temporal addition is impossible for God.

Core Idea
All events trace back to celestial motion; God knows them in one timeless unveiling.

The philosophers generalize the eclipse example to all temporal events. They argue that every event has a cause, and the causal chain ultimately leads to the circular motion of the heavens. God knows this entire chain in a single, timeless knowledge. This lesson unpacks the causal hierarchy and the final conclusion that knowledge requiring temporal indexation is impossible for God.

Guided Path
1
Read the causal chain
Read the passage describing how all events are linked through causes to the circular motion of the heavens.
The chain: events → other causes → circular motion → heavens → souls' desire to resemble God.
2
Understand the nature of God's knowledge
God knows all of this in a single unveiling that is proportionate and unaffected by time.
The phrase 'انكشافاً واحداً متناسباً' indicates a unified, non-temporal knowledge.
3
Grasp the final restriction
Everything that requires temporal addition (e.g., 'now') cannot be known by God because it would necessitate change.
This is the crux: God cannot know events as 'present' or 'past' in a temporal sense.
4
Reflect on the implication
The philosophers thereby deny that God has particular knowledge of temporal events as they occur in time.
This conclusion was later refuted by Ghazali.

According to the philosophers, why can God not know that the eclipse exists now?

D) Because the eclipse is not caused by God.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers conclude that God's knowledge is timeless and universal, not indexed to temporal moments, because temporal indexation would entail change in the immutable divine essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Do I live as if God's knowledge is limited to the past and present, or do I acknowledge that He sees my every intention and future action in a single eternal moment? What would it mean for me to act with that certainty?
?
The philosophers say that temporal indexation would imply change in God. But in my heart, do I sometimes imagine God 'reacting' to my prayers or deeds as if He learns something new? How can I train my soul to reverence His timeless knowledge?

God's Knowledge of Individuals: The Philosophers' View
Ghazali explains the philosophers' view on God's knowledge of individuals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that God knows only universals, not individual persons, and why this principle, if accepted, would uproot the foundations of religious law.

Core Idea
God knows humanity, not individuals — a principle that uproots religion.

In this passage, Ghazali presents the philosophers' doctrine on divine knowledge. They claim that God knows only universal concepts, not specific individuals. Ghazali then warns that this principle destroys the foundation of religious law, which depends on God's knowledge of each person's deeds.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Ghazali first extends the earlier argument (time) to matter and place, then states the philosophers' core claim about individuals.
The passage ends abruptly with 'إذ مضمونها' — what follows is an editor's note.
2
Identify the philosophers' claim
Find where they say 'لا يعلم عوارض زيد وعمرو وخالد' and what they affirm instead. Note that they claim God knows 'الإنسان المطلق' and all its attributes universally.
They do not deny knowledge of individuals entirely; they say it is universal, not particular.
3
Understand why distinction requires sense
Learn why the philosophers think distinguishing individuals (like Zayd and Amr) requires sense perception and spatial reference ('الإشارة إلى جهة معينة'). The intellect can only grasp universals.
The argument hinges on the nature of 'this' and 'that' as sense-relative.
4
See the consequence for God
Reflect on why this distinction is impossible for God: He has no body, no sense organs, and no spatial location, so He cannot refer to individuals as 'this' or 'that'.
5
Grasp the impact on religious law
Ghazali concludes that this principle 'استأصلوا بها الشرائع بالكلية' — it uproots all religious laws. Think about why: reward and punishment require God to know each individual's specific actions.
This is a crucial point where Ghazali ties philosophical doctrine to practical religion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا فيما ينقسم بالزمان، وكذا مذهبهم فيما ينقسم بالمادة والمكان، كأشخاص الناس والحيوانات، فإنهم يقولون: لا يعلم عوارض زيد وعمرو وخالد، وإنما يعلم الإنسان المطلق بعلم كلي، ويعلم عوارضه، وخواصه، وأنه ينبغي أن يكون بدنه مركباً من أعضاء، بعضها للبطش، وبعضها للمشي، وبعضها للإدراك، وبعضها زوج، وبعضها فرد، وأن قواه ينبغي أن تكون مبثوثة في أجزائه، وهلم جرا، إلى كل صفة في خارج الآدمي وباطنه، وكل ما هو من لواحقه، وصفاته، ولوازمه، حتى لا يعزب عن علمه شيء، ويعلمه كليا
فأما شخص زيد، فإنما يتميز عن شخص عمرو، للحس لا للعقل، فإن عماد التمييز إليه الإشارة إلى جهة معينة، والعقل يعقل الجهة المطلقة الكلية والمكان الكلي، فأما قولنا: « هذا » و « هذا » فهو إشارة إلى نسبة حاصلة لذلك المحسوس، إلى الحاس، لكونه منه على قرب، أو بعد، أو جهة معينة، وذلك يستحيل في حقه
وهذه قاعدة اعتقدوها، واستأصلوا بها الشرائع بالكلية، إذ مضمونها
This is regarding what is divided by time, and likewise their doctrine regarding what is divided by matter and place, like individual persons of people and animals. For they say: He does not know the accidents of Zayd, Amr, and Khalid, but only knows the absolute human with a universal knowledge, and knows its accidental attributes, its properties, and that his body should be composed of limbs, some for striking, some for walking, some for perception, some paired, some single, and that his powers should be distributed in his parts, and so on, up to every attribute in the exterior of man and his interior, and everything that belongs to its concomitants, attributes, and necessary properties, so that nothing escapes His knowledge, and He knows it universally. As for the individual Zayd, he is distinguished from the individual Amr only by sense, not by intellect, for the basis of distinction is pointing to a specific direction, and the intellect only comprehends the absolute universal direction and the universal place. As for our saying 'this' and 'this', it is an indication of a relation occurring in that sense-perceptible thing to the sense, due to its being near or far or in a specific direction relative to it, and that is impossible with respect to Him. And this is a principle they believed, by which they utterly uprooted the religious laws, for its content...
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' doctrine concerning God's knowledge of individuals (particularly humans and animals) as opposed to temporal events. They apply the same logic to things divided by matter and place: God does not know the specific accidents of individual people like Zayd, Amr, and Khalid. Instead, He knows only the universal 'human' and its essential and accidental attributes—composition of limbs, powers, etc.—all in a universal manner. Individual distinction (e.g., Zayd vs. Amr) is only apprehended by sense perception, not by intellect, because it involves pointing to a specific spatial direction. Intellect can only grasp universal direction and universal place. The phrases 'this' and 'that' refer to a relation between the sense-perceptible object and the perceiver, based on proximity, distance, or direction—all of which are impossible for God, who is not embodied and not subject to spatial relations. Ghazali then states his own conclusion: this principle, if accepted, completely uproots the religious laws (Sharia) because it denies God's particular knowledge of individuals, which is crucial for reward and punishment based on specific deeds.

Key Terms
كلي
جزئي
حس
عقل
إشارة
جهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the philosophers' view means God is ignorant of individuals.
Correction: The philosophers claim God knows individuals universally, not as distinct individuals. They do not deny all knowledge, only particular knowledge dependent on sense and spatial reference.

Checkpoint
Why, according to the philosophers, does God not know Zayd as distinct from Amr?
A) Because Zayd and Amr are not real.
B) Because distinction requires sense perception and spatial reference, which are impossible for God.
C) Because God only cares about universals.
D) Because God's knowledge is limited to celestial events.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' denial of God's particular knowledge of individuals, if accepted, would undermine the basis for reward and punishment in religious law.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God only knew you as a generic human, not as you uniquely are, would you still feel accountable for your personal sins? How does the philosophers' view make the concept of divine judgment meaningless for you individually?
?
The philosophers say God cannot say 'this' and 'this' about you. Do you live as though God sees every detail of your life? If not, what would change if you truly believed He does not know your specific actions?
?
Ghazali says this principle 'uproots the religious laws entirely.' Are there any beliefs you hold that, if truly examined, would similarly undermine your sense of personal responsibility before God? What are they?

Three Types of Change in Attributes and Their Implications
Ghazali classifies three types of change in attributes and their implications for immutable beings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's classification of three types of change in attributes and why only the second type (change in external relations) can apply to an immutable being like God.

Core Idea
Only external relations change without affecting the essence; attribute changes require a mutable subject.

Ghazali begins by clarifying that attributes can change in different ways. He identifies three distinct types: change in the attribute itself, change in relation only, and change in both attribute and relation. This classification is crucial for understanding why God, who is immutable, can still know particular changing events without His essence changing.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three types
Read the Arabic and translation. Underline the three types described by Ghazali: the first (like color change), the second (like power to move), and the third (like knowledge of a new object).
2
Understand the first type (essential change)
The first type involves a transformation of an inherent attribute, e.g., white becoming black. This type changes the essence itself and cannot apply to an immutable being.
Note that the attribute itself is replaced, not just its relation.
3
Analyze the second type (relational change)
The second type involves change only in external relations, not in the essence. Example: a being's ability to move a particular object when that object ceases to exist. The underlying power remains unchanged.
Ghazali emphasizes that power is a single attribute with multiple possible relations.
4
Examine the third type and conclusion
The third type involves change in both the attribute and its relation, as in acquiring new knowledge of a newly existing object. This also cannot apply to God. The conclusion: only the second type may occur in an immutable being.
Knowledge of a particular requires a new mental disposition, unlike power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيه وإشارة:
قد تتغير الصفات للأشياء على وجوه:
منها مثل أن يسود الذي كان أبيض، وذلك باستحالة صفة متقررة، غير مضافة.
ومنها مثل أن يكون الشيء قادراً على تحريك ما، فلو عدم ذلك الجسم، استحال أن يقال: إنه قادر على تحريكه، فاستحال إذن هو عن صفته، ولكن من غير تغير في ذاته، بل في إضافته، فإن كونه قادراً صفة له واحدة، تلحقها إضافة إلى أمر كلي، من تحريك أجسام بحال ما مثلاً، لزوماً أولياً ذاتياً، ويدخل في ذلك زيد، وعمرو، وحجارة، وشجرة، دخولاً ثانياً. فإنه ليس كونه قادراً متعلقاً به الإضافات المعينة تعلق ما لا بد منه، فإنه لو لم يكن زيد أصلاً في الإمكان، ولم تقع إضافة القوة إلى تحريكه أبداً، ما ضر ذلك في كونه قادراً على التحريك. فإذن أصل كونه قادراً لا يتغير بتغير أحوال المقدور عليه من الأشياء، بل إنما تتغير الإضافات الخارجة فقط.
فهذا القسم كالمقابل للذي قبله.
ومنها مثل أن يكون الشيء عالماً بأن شيئاً ليس، ثم يحدث الشيء، فيصير عالماً بأن الشيء، فتتغير الإضافة والصفة المضافة معاً، فإن كونه عالماً بشيء ما تختص الإضافة به، حتى إنه إذا كان عالماً بمعنى كلي، لم يكف ذلك أن يكون عالماً بجزئي جزئي، بل يكون العلم بالنتيجة علماً مستأنفاً، تلزمه إضافة مستأنفة، وهيأة للنفس مستجدة، لها إضافة مستجدة مخصوصة، غير العلم بالمقدمة، وغير هيأة تحققها، لا كما كان في كونه قادراً له بهيئة واحدة إضافات شتى. فهذا إذا اختلف حال المضاف إليه من عدم ووجود، وجب أن يختلف حال الشيء الذي له الصفة، لا في إضافة الصفة نفسها فقط بل في الصفة التي تلزمها تلك الإضافة أيضاً. فما ليس موضوعاً للتغير، لم يجز أن يعرض له تبدل بحسب القسم الأول، ولا بحسب القسم الثالث. وأما بحسب القسم الثاني فقد يجوز في إضافات بعيدة لا تؤثر في الذات.
Admonition and indication:
Attributes change in things in several ways:
Among them is like something becoming black that was white, and that is by the transformation of an established non-relative attribute.
Among them is like a thing being able to move something; if that body ceased to exist, it cannot be said that it is able to move it, so it is therefore changed from its attribute, but without change in its essence, rather in its relation. For its being able is a single attribute which is accompanied by a relation to a universal matter, like moving bodies in a certain state, as a primary essential concomitance, and into it Zayd, Amr, stones, and a tree enter secondarily. For its being able is not related to particular relations in a necessary way; if Zayd had never existed in possibility, and the relation of power to moving him never occurred, that would not harm its being able to move. So the basis of its being able does not change with the change of the states of the object of power among things; rather only the external relations change.
This section is like the opposite of the one before it.
Among them is like a thing knowing that something is not, then the thing occurs, so it becomes knowing that the thing [exists]; so both the relation and the related attribute change together. For its being knowing of a particular thing is specifically related to it, so much so that if it knows by a universal meaning, that is not sufficient for it to know a particular particular, but knowledge of the result is a new knowledge, requiring a new relation and a new disposition of the soul, having a specific new relation, different from knowledge of the premise and the disposition of its verification, not like in the case of being able, where it has one disposition with multiple relations. So when the state of the thing related to changes from non-existence to existence, the state of the thing that has the attribute must change, not only in the relation of the attribute itself but also in the attribute that accompanies that relation. Therefore, what is not subject to change cannot undergo change according to the first type nor according to the third type. As for the second type, change may occur in remote relations that do not affect the essence.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage is a classification of three types of change in attributes, with implications for immutable beings like God. Ghazali first introduces an admonition and indication that attributes change in several ways. 

First type: change in the attribute itself (like color). The substance transforms from white to black, involving a change in an inherent, non-relative quality.

Second type: change in relation only, not in essence. Example: a thing is able to move something; if that body ceases to exist, the ability to move it is lost, but the agent's power as a capability remains unchanged—only the external relation to that particular body disappears. Power is a single attribute that relates to universal motions, and specific objects are included secondarily. The core ability does not depend on any particular object; thus, when the object changes, only the relations change, not the attribute itself. Ghazali notes that this type is opposite to the first (change in essence).

Third type: change in both the relation and the attribute itself. Example: knowledge of a particular thing that was non-existent becomes existent. When the object changes from non-existence to existence, the knower acquires new knowledge—a new attribute and a new relation. Unlike power, knowledge of a particular requires a specific mental disposition tied to that particular. The knowledge of the result is distinct from knowledge of the premise. So here, both the attribute (the state of knowledge) and its relation to the object change.

Conclusion: For something not subject to change (like the Necessary Existent), it cannot undergo the first type (change in essential quality) or the third type (change in attribute plus relation). It may only undergo the second type—changes in remote external relations that do not affect the essence. This classification serves to argue that God's knowledge of changing particulars does not entail change in His essence.

Key Terms
تغير الصفات
إضافة
صفة متقررة
ذات
قادر
عالم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any change in relation implies a change in essence or attribute.
Correction: Ghazali shows that in the second type, only external relations change without affecting the essence or core attribute.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali's classification, which type of change involves both the attribute itself and its relation changing?
A) The first type (e.g., becoming black)
B) The second type (e.g., ability to move)
C) The third type (e.g., knowledge of a new object)
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's classification shows that only relational changes (type two) can occur in an immutable being, preserving divine transcendence while allowing for knowledge of changing events.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When something new happens in your life, do you unconsciously assume God's knowledge changes? How does Ghazali's classification force you to reconsider the nature of God's attributes? Will you commit to not attributing change to God's essence in your speech and thoughts from now on?
?
You know that your own power and knowledge are constantly changing. Does this lesson make you feel small before an immutable God? In what specific way can you cultivate deeper reverence for His unchanging nature today?
?
If only relational changes can occur in God, does that make His connection to your life feel distant or real? Reflect: Does your faith rest on a God who changes according to your circumstances, or on the One who remains constant? What must change in your understanding of God to align with this truth?

Pure Relation vs. State with Relation
Ghazali distinguishes pure relations from states with concomitant relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between a pure relation and a relational state, and why God's attributes like power and knowledge are not mere relations.

Core Idea
God's attributes are real states, not just relations—they entail relations without being reducible to them.

This short but profound passage from Ghazali tackles a crucial nuance in the philosophy of attributes. Before we can discuss how God's knowledge relates to changing particulars, we need a clear taxonomy of relations. Ghazali offers two crisp examples to show the difference between a pure, dependent relation and a real attribute that carries a relation. Let's read his subtle point.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English translation carefully. Notice the contrast Ghazali sets up between 'being right/left' and 'being powerful/knowing'.
2
Identify the two categories
Find the description of 'pure relation' and 'relational state' in the passage. What does Ghazali say about each?
3
Apply to divine attributes
Reflect on why this distinction matters for theology. If God's power and knowledge were pure relations, they would be accidental. But Ghazali asserts they are settled states that necessarily involve relations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نكتة:
كونك يميناً وشمالاً إضافة محضة، وكونك قادراً وعالماً هو كونك في حال متقررة في نفسك تتبعها إضافة لازمة أو لاحقة، فأنت بهما ذو حال مضافة، لا ذو إضافة محضة.
Subtle point:
Your being right and left is a pure relation, and your being powerful and knowing is your being in a settled state in yourself followed by a necessary or subsequent relation, so you are by them possessor of a relational state, not possessor of a pure relation.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage is a subtle point (nukta) that clarifies a distinction: pure relations versus states that carry relations. Ghazali gives two examples: being 'right' or 'left' is a pure relation—it depends entirely on position relative to something else and does not involve a real attribute in the essence. In contrast, being 'powerful' (qādir) or 'knowing' (ʿālim) is not a mere relation; it is a real, settled state (ḥāl mutaqarrara) in the self. This state inherently brings with it a relation—either necessarily (lāzima) or subsequently (lāḥiqa). Therefore, a powerful or knowing being is described as having a 'relational state' (dhū ḥāl muḍāfa), not a pure relation. This nuance matters for theology: God's attributes like power and knowledge are real, not mere relations, but they do involve relations to objects. The distinction helps explain how God's knowledge of changing particulars does not imply mutation in His essence.

Key Terms
إضافة محضة
حال مضافة
قادر
عالم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's attributes like power and knowledge are merely relations to objects, similar to being 'right' or 'left'.
Correction: Ghazali clarifies that these attributes are real states in God's essence that entail relations—they are not reducible to pure relations.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, which of the following is an example of a pure relation?
A) Being powerful
B) Being knowing
C) Being right or left
D) Being a substance

Key Takeaway
God's attributes like power and knowledge are real, intrinsic states that necessarily involve relations—they are not mere relative concepts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe yourself as 'powerful' or 'knowing', do you see these as intrinsic realities that belong to you, or do you only feel these attributes when compared to others? How does this distinction affect your sense of self-worth?
?
Ghazali says that being 'right' or 'left' says nothing about who you really are. What 'pure relations' have you mistakenly taken as defining your identity—your job, your status, your relationships? Can you list them and commit to remembering that they are not your real state?
?
If God's attributes like power and knowledge are real states and not just relations, what does that mean for how you trust Him? Are you tempted to think His power is only relevant when He acts, or can you rest in the reality of His perfections even when you don't see their effects?

The Necessary Existent's Knowledge is Non-Temporal
Ghazali concludes that the Necessary Existent's knowledge of particulars is not temporal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that the Necessary Existent's knowledge of particulars must be non-temporal and how it relates to His eternal decree.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is timeless: He knows all particulars in a single, eternal act.

In this appendix, Ghazali draws a crucial theological conclusion: God's knowledge of particular events cannot be like human knowledge, which changes with time. Instead, His knowledge is eternal and unchanging, transcending past, present, and future. Let's explore his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp its rhythm. Then read the English translation to understand the literal meaning.
Notice the contrast between 'temporal knowledge' and 'the holy and exalted manner'.
2
Identify the two main claims
The passage makes two key points: first, a negation (knowledge is not temporal), and second, an affirmation (God knows all things necessarily). Find both.
3
Reflect on the reasoning
Why would temporal knowledge imply change in God's essence? Consider how past, present, and future would require different 'moments' of knowing.
This connects to the core idea of divine immutability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تذنيب:
فالواجب الوجود يجب ألا يكون علمه بالجزئيات علماً زمانياً، حتى يدخل فيه الآن والماضي والمستقبل، فيعرض لصفة ذاته أن تتغير، بل يجب أن يكون علمه بالجزئيات على الوجه المقدس العالي على الزمان والدهر.
ويجب أن يكون عالماً بكل شيء، لأن كل شيء لازم بوسط أو بغير وسط، يتأدى إليه بعينه قدره، الذي هو تفصيل قضائه الأول، تأدياً واجباً، إذ كان ما لا يجب لا يكون.
Appendix:
The Necessary Existent must not have knowledge of particulars as temporal knowledge, such that the now, past, and future enter into it, and the attribute of His essence undergoes change; rather, His knowledge of particulars must be in the holy and exalted manner, above time and duration.
And He must be knowing of everything, because everything, whether mediated or unmediated, necessarily reaches Him exactly as His decree, which is the detail of His first determination, a necessary reaching, since that which is not necessary does not exist.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage is an appendix (tadhnīb) that draws the theological conclusion from the previous discussion on change in attributes. Ghazali states that the Necessary Existent (wājib al-wujūd, i.e., God) cannot have temporal knowledge of particulars (juzʾiyyāt) — that is, knowledge that changes with time, involving past, present, and future as distinct moments. Such knowledge would entail change in the divine essence, which is impossible. Instead, God's knowledge of particulars must be on a holy and exalted level that transcends time (zamān) and duration (dahr). It is eternal and unchanging. 

Then Ghazali adds that God must know everything, because everything that exists (whether directly or indirectly) necessarily follows from His decree (qaḍāʾ). The 'decree' is the first determination (al-qaḍāʾ al-awwal), and all things unfold from it necessarily. Since whatever is necessary exists, and whatever is not necessary does not exist, everything that does exist is a necessary consequence of God's knowledge and will. Therefore, God knows all things eternally in a single, timeless act.

Key Terms
واجب الوجود
علم بالجزئيات
زماني
دهر
قضاء أول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's knowledge of particulars changes as time passes, like human knowledge.
Correction: Ghazali argues that God's knowledge is eternal and unchanging, above time and duration.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, how does the Necessary Existent know particulars?
A) As temporal knowledge with past, present, future
B) As universal knowledge without any relation to particulars
C) As non-temporal knowledge above time and duration
D) As knowledge that changes as particulars change

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge of particulars is non-temporal and encompasses all things eternally.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's knowledge of my entire life—past, present, and future—is a single eternal 'now', do I truly believe that my worst failures and best successes are all present before Him without change? Does this make me feel exposed or secure? How would I live differently today if I really felt that gaze?
?
The passage says everything necessarily reaches God's decree. If every detail of my life is already decreed and known, what is the point of my dua' (supplication)? Do I pray as if my prayers can change what is decreed, or do I pray with the confidence that even my prayers were decreed? Examine your heart: is there a part of you that doubts the efficacy of prayer because of divine foreknowledge?
?
Human knowledge changes as we learn—today I might think I know myself, but tomorrow I discover new weaknesses. God's knowledge of me never changes. Does that thought give you comfort or anxiety? In what area of your life do you most need to accept that God's unchanging knowledge of you is more accurate than your own self-assessment?

Razi Confirms Ibn Sina's Conclusion on Divine Knowledge
Razi explains Ibn Sina's conclusion on divine knowledge of particulars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Razi confirmed that Ibn Sina's true conclusion was that God's knowledge of particulars is non-temporal and unchanging.

Core Idea
Razi confirms: Ibn Sina's God knows particulars without time or change.

This lesson presents a key commentary by Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi on Ibn Sina's argument about divine knowledge. You have seen Ghazali's interpretation; here Razi puts the conclusion beyond doubt. By examining his words, we see that Ibn Sina's position is not that God lacks knowledge of particulars, but that His knowledge is eternal and unaffected by temporal change.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that it begins with an editorial note before presenting Razi's commentary. Focus on the quote from Razi.
The bracketed portion is Razi's direct commentary.
2
Identify the two ways of knowing
Razi mentions that Ibn Sina clarified two ways of knowing particulars: one that entails change and one that does not. Identify these two ways in the passage.
3
Understand the conclusion
Since change is impossible for God, Ibn Sina concluded that God must know particulars in the second, non-temporal manner. Make sure you see the logical flow: two possibilities, elimination of one, affirmation of the other.
This conclusion is exactly what Ghazali understood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا هو نص « ابن سينا » الذي يتحدث الغزالي عنه وعن « الفارابي » حين يقرر هذه القاعدة، وقد عرفت فهم الغزالي فيه، وقد وافقه في هذا الفهم كثيرون، نذكر من بينهم الإمام « الرازي » شارح الإشارات حيث يقول:
[ لما فرغ من بيان أن الجزئيات كيف تعلم حتى يلزم التغير، وكيف تعلم حتى لا يلزم التغير، وكان التغير على واجب الوجود ممتنعاً، صرح في هذا الفصل بالنتيجة، فقال يجب ألا يكون عالماً بالجزئيات علماً زمانياً متغيراً، ويجب أن يكون عالماً بها على الوجه الثاني، الذي لا يتغير بتغير الزمان ].
This is the text of Ibn Sina about whom Ghazali speaks, and about al-Farabi, when he establishes this principle. You have known Ghazali's understanding of it, and many have agreed with him in this understanding, among them we mention Imam al-Razi, commentator of al-Isharat, where he says:
[ After he finished clarifying how particulars are known such that change is entailed, and how they are known such that change is not entailed, and since change is impossible for the Necessary Existent, he stated in this chapter the conclusion, saying: He must not know particulars with a temporal changing knowledge, and He must know them in the second manner, which does not change with the change of time ].
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage presents a commentary by Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, a major Ash'ari theologian and commentator on Ibn Sina's al-Isharat. The editorial note first identifies this as the text of Ibn Sina that Ghazali discusses (along with al-Farabi). It notes that Ghazali's understanding of the argument is well known and that many scholars agree with him. Al-Razi is cited as one of those who confirm the conclusion. 

Al-Razi's commentary summarizes Ibn Sina's argument: Ibn Sina first explained how particulars can be known in two ways—one that entails change (temporal knowledge) and one that does not (eternal knowledge). Since change is impossible for the Necessary Existent, Ibn Sina concluded that God's knowledge of particulars must be of the second type: non-temporal and unchanging. Al-Razi thus affirms that Ibn Sina's intended conclusion is exactly what Ghazali understood: God's knowledge is not temporal.

Misconception: Ibn Sina believed God's knowledge of particulars is temporal and changes.
Correction: Razi explains that Ibn Sina's conclusion is the opposite: God's knowledge is non-temporal and unchanging.

Key Takeaway
Razi's commentary confirms that Ibn Sina concluded God's knowledge of particulars is eternal and unchanging, matching Ghazali's understanding.

The Tusi-Shirazi Debate on Divine Knowledge of Particulars
Tusi objects to Ibn Sina's reasoning as juristic, offers an alternative, and Shirazi begins a defense.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Tusi's objection to Ibn Sina's reasoning and Shirazi's attempted defense, and the distinction between sensory and intellectual knowledge of particulars.

Core Idea
God's knowledge of particulars is intellectual, not sensory.

This passage presents a critical moment in the debate over divine knowledge of changing particulars. Tusi objects that Ibn Sina's argument resembles juristic particularization and offers an alternative proof based on the intellect-senses distinction. Shirazi responds, though his full defense is not preserved here, claiming that Tusi misunderstood Ibn Sina.

Guided Path
1
Read Tusi's objection
Read Tusi's critique from 'Know that this style' to 'perceive them in the second manner'. Note how he compares Ibn Sina's reasoning to juristic particularization.
Tusi is not rejecting the conclusion but the method.
2
Understand Tusi's alternative
Tusi proposes that knowledge of the cause necessitates knowledge of the effect, but not sensory perception. Changing particulars are known intellectually, not through senses.
This avoids the problem of change in God.
3
Consider Shirazi's defense (incomplete)
Read the editorial note and Shirazi's opening sentence. He argues Tusi's objection is based on his own understanding, not Ibn Sina's intended meaning. Note that the text cuts off.
The full defense is not provided in this excerpt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً « الطوسي » حيث يقول، تعليقاً على النص، شارحاً ومعترضاً:
[ واعلم أن هذه السياقة تشبه سياقة الفقهاء، في تخصيص بعض الأحكام العامة بأحكام تعارضها في الظاهر، وذلك لأن الحكم بأن العلم بالعلة يوجب العلم بالمعلول، إن لم يكن كلياً، لم يمكن أن يحكم بإحاطة الواجب بالكل؛ وإن كان كلياً، وكان الجزئي المتغير من جملة معلولاته، أوجب ذلك الحكم أن يكون عالماً به لا محالة. فالقول بأنه لا يجوز أن يكون عالماً به لامتناع كون الواجب موضوعاً للتغير، تخصيص لذلك الحكم الكلي بحكم آخر عارضه في بعض الصور، وهذا دأب الفقهاء ومن يجرى مجراهم. ولا يجوز أمثال ذلك في المباحث المعقولة، لامتناع تعارض الأحكام فيها. فالصواب أن يؤخذ بيان يقع هذا المطلوب من مأخذ آخر، وهو أن يقال: العلم بالعلة يوجب العلم بالمعلول، ولا يوجب الإحساس به، وإدراك الجزئيات المتغيرة من حيث هي متغيرة لا يمكن إلا بالآلات الجسمانية كالحواس وما يجرى مجراها، والمدرك بذلك الإدراك يكون موضوعاً للتغير لا محالة، أما إدراكها على الوجه الكلي فلا يمكن إلا أن يدرك بالعقل، والمدرك بهذا الإدراك يمكن ألا يكون موضوعاً للتغير. فإذن الواجب الأول وكل ما لا يكون موضوعاً للتغير، بل كل ما هو عاقل، يمتنع أن يدركها - من جهة ما هو عاقل - على الوجه الأول، ويجب أن يدركها على الوجه الثاني ].
هو فهم « الغزالي » و « الرازي » و « الطوسي » في النص ولكنه غير متعين فيه، بل هناك ذاك لسواه، « فالشيرازي » صاحب ( المحاكمات ) يقول:
{ إن اعتراضه - يعني الطوسي - وارد على فهمه هو من كلام الشيخ - يعني « ابن سينا » - لا على مراد الشيخ، كما حققناه، لما أن العلم بالجزئيات المتغيرة ...
And also al-Tusi where he says, commenting on the text, explaining and objecting:
[ Know that this style resembles the style of jurists, in particularizing some general rulings with rulings that apparently contradict them. That is because the judgment that knowledge of the cause necessitates knowledge of the effect, if it is not universal, then one cannot judge that the Necessary encompasses all; but if it is universal, and the changing particular is among His effects, then that judgment necessitates that He knows it necessarily. So the statement that He cannot know it because the Necessary cannot be subject to change is a particularization of that universal judgment by another judgment that conflicts with it in some cases, and this is the way of jurists and those who follow their method. Such things are not permissible in rational investigations, because conflict of judgments therein is impossible. So the correct approach is to take a proof for this desired conclusion from another source, which is to say: knowledge of the cause necessitates knowledge of the effect, but does not necessitate sensory perception of it; and perception of changing particulars insofar as they are changing is impossible except through corporeal instruments like the senses and what resembles them, and the perceiver by that perception is necessarily subject to change; as for perceiving them in the universal manner, it can only be perceived by the intellect, and the perceiver by this perception can be not subject to change. Therefore, the First Necessary and everything that is not subject to change — rather everything that is rational — it is impossible for it to perceive them, insofar as it is rational, in the first manner, and it must perceive them in the second manner ].
This is the understanding of al-Ghazali, al-Razi, and al-Tusi of the text, but it is not determined therein; rather there is another meaning besides it. Al-Shirazi, author of al-Muhakamat, says:
{ His objection — meaning al-Tusi's — is directed at his own understanding of the Shaykh's words — meaning Ibn Sina — not at the Shaykh's intended meaning, as we have verified, because knowledge of changing particulars ...
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage presents a critical objection by Nasir al-Din al-Tusi to Ibn Sina's argument as presented earlier, and a defense by Qutb al-Din al-Shirazi. Tusi comments as both explainer and objector. He compares Ibn Sina's reasoning to the method of jurists (fuqahāʾ) who particularize general rulings with conflicting rulings in apparent cases. Specifically, Tusi argues: The general principle 'knowledge of the cause necessitates knowledge of the effect' must be universal; otherwise, we cannot affirm that God knows all things. But if it is universal, then since changing particulars are effects of God, God must know them. The claim that God cannot know them because change is impossible for Him is an unjustified particularization of the universal principle with a conflicting rule, which is like juristic reasoning and unacceptable in rational matters. Because such contradictions are impossible in necessary truths. So Tusi proposes an alternative: knowledge of the cause necessitates knowledge of the effect, but not sensory perception of it. Perception of changing particulars as changing requires bodily organs and entails change. But intellectual perception in a universal manner does not entail change. So God, being non-corporeal and immutable, perceives particulars intellectually in a universal way. This, Tusi says, is the proper proof.

Then an editorial comment notes that this is the understanding of Ghazali, Razi, and Tusi, but it is not the only interpretation. Al-Shirazi, author of al-Muhakamat, responds that Tusi's objection is against his own understanding of Ibn Sina's words, not against Ibn Sina's actual intended meaning. Shirazi claims to have verified that Ibn Sina's intended meaning is different. The quote from Shirazi is cut off at 'knowledge of changing particulars...' but the context shows he defends Ibn Sina against Tusi's criticism.

Key Terms

الشيرازي
تخصيص
علم بالعلة
إحساس
عاقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Tusi completely rejects Ibn Sina's claim that God knows particulars.
Correction: Tusi does not reject the conclusion; he offers an alternative proof that avoids the juristic analogy and preserves God's knowledge of particulars in an intellectual manner.

Checkpoint
What does Tusi accuse Ibn Sina's argument of resembling?
A) Mathematical proofs
B) Juristic particularization
C) Sophistical refutations
D) Poetic reasoning

Key Takeaway
The debate highlights the distinction between sensory and intellectual knowledge, affirming that God knows particulars intellectually without change.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God knows every sin you have committed—past, present, and future—without His essence being affected or changed? How does that certainty—or lack of it—shape your repentance and hope for forgiveness?
?
Tusi distinguishes between knowing a changing thing intellectually and sensing it emotionally. When you think of God's knowledge of your most shameful moments, do you imagine Him as a distant observer unaffected, or does your shame assume He feels disgust? Which belief aligns with the lesson?
?
If God's knowledge of your heart's every shift is purely intellectual and not experienced in Him, how does that change your view of being ‘seen’ by Him? Does it make you more careful, or more careless, knowing His knowing does not change Him?

Wajib al-Wujud's Knowledge of Particulars is Non-Temporal
Main assertion that God's knowledge of particulars is non-temporal and universal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Necessary Being's knowledge of particulars must be timeless and universal, not subject to temporal change.

Core Idea
God's knowledge of particulars is above time, not bound by past, present, or future.

In this appendix, al-Ghazali presents a key doctrine from Ibn Sina regarding divine knowledge. The passage distinguishes temporal, changing knowledge from God's transcendent, eternal knowledge. As you read, notice how the argument moves from a negation to an affirmation, grounded in the concept of divine decree.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Pay attention to the key terms and the flow of the argument.
The passage begins with a negation and then provides the correct positive description.
2
Identify the negative and positive claims
Find where the author denies temporal knowledge and where he affirms the mode of knowledge. Underline these parts.
The negative claim is about 'ilman zamaniyyan'; the positive uses 'al-wajh al-muqaddas al-'ali'.
3
Understand the justification
The passage justifies universal knowledge by linking everything to God's decree. Reflect on why this makes knowledge timeless.
Consider the phrase 'tafsil qada'ihi al-awwal'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تذنيب : فالواجب الوجود ، يجب ألا يكون علمه بالجزئيات ، علماً زمانياً، حتى يدخل فيه ، الآن والماضي ، والمستقبل ، فيعرض لصفة ذاته، أن تتغير ، بل يجب أن يكون علمه بالجزئيات ، على الوجه المقدس العالى ، على الزمان والدهر • ويجب أن يكون عالماً بكل شيء ، لأن كل شيء لازم بوسط ، أو بغير وسط ، يتأدى إليه بعينه قدره ، الذي هو تفصيل قضائه الأول ، تأدياً واجباً ، إذ كان ما لا يجب لا يكون كما علمت ] الفصل الثامن عشر وما بعده من النمط السابع ، طبع دار المعارف هذا هو نص « ابن سينا » الذى يتحدث الغزالى عنه وعن « الفارابى » حين يقرر هذه القاعدة
Appendix: The Necessary Being must not have knowledge of particulars as temporal knowledge, such that the present, past, and future enter into it, for then His essential attribute would change. Rather, His knowledge of particulars must be in the sacred and exalted manner, transcending time and eternity. And He must be knowledgeable of everything, for everything, whether mediated or unmediated, necessarily reaches Him as His very decree, which is the elaboration of His first decree, necessary to reach Him, since what is not necessary does not exist, as you have been informed. This is the text of Ibn Sina that al-Ghazali discusses, concerning him and al-Farabi, when he establishes this principle.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal assertion about God's knowledge. It begins by stating that the Necessary Being (wajib al-wujud) cannot have temporal knowledge of particulars, because that would introduce change into His essence—a past, present, and future. Instead, His knowledge must be on a sacred and exalted level, above time and eternity. Then it asserts that He knows everything because everything, whether through a means or not, necessarily reaches Him as His own decree, which is the unfolding of His first decree. The final sentence identifies this as the text of Ibn Sina that al-Ghazali discusses regarding al-Farabi's formulation. The argument is that God's knowledge is timeless and universal, not contingent on temporal sequence.

Key Terms
واجب الوجود
الجزئيات
علماً زمانياً
الوجه المقدس العالي
قضائه الأول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think this denies God's knowledge of particulars altogether.
Correction: It affirms a timeless knowledge, not a denial.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, how must the Necessary Being's knowledge of particulars be?
A) Temporal and changing
B) Non-temporal and exalted above time
C) Limited to universal concepts
D) Primarily concerned with the past

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge of all things is timeless and universal, grounded in His eternal decree.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God already knows every detail of your future without it changing His knowledge, does that make you feel helpless or liberated? Why?
?
When you sin, do you act as though God is surprised? How can the truth that His knowledge is timeless and unchanging change the way you repent?
?
God's knowledge of your past, present, and future is a single, eternal act. Does this reality make you more mindful of His presence now, or do you live as if He only sees your present moment? What tiny change can you make today to align your actions with this truth?

Razi and Tusi on Divine Knowledge of Particulars
Two commentators on divine knowledge: summary and alternative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi summarizes the conclusion that God knows particulars in an unchanging manner and how al-Tusi critiques the reasoning and offers an alternative based on the distinction between intellectual and sensory perception.

Core Idea
God knows changing particulars not by sensory perception but by a universal, unchanging intellectual knowledge.

Al-Razi and al-Tusi both address how God can know particulars without change. Al-Razi simply states the conclusion, while al-Tusi objects to the method of specifying general rules and instead argues that God's knowledge is intellectual, not sensory. This passage shows the evolution of the argument from a straightforward conclusion to a refined epistemological distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage overview
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the translation. Notice the two distinct voices: al-Razi and al-Tusi.
The passage contains two commentators; let their own words come through first.
2
Identify al-Razi's point
Find where al-Razi restates the conclusion about two kinds of knowledge (temporal vs unchanging). He is summarizing what was proven earlier.
Al-Razi does not add new argument; he clarifies the result.
3
Analyze al-Tusi's objection
Al-Tusi critiques the reasoning used by al-Ghazali (and others) as resembling juristic specification. Understand why he thinks this method is inappropriate for rational proofs.
Notice the contrast between juristic and rational methods.
4
Understand the alternative argument
Al-Tusi offers a different proof: knowledge of the cause gives knowledge of the effect but not sensory perception. Changing particulars require senses; God, being purely intellectual, knows them universally without change.
This is the key insight: separating knowledge from sensation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد عرفت فهم الغزالى فيه ، وقد وافقه في هذا الفهم كثيرون ، نذكر من بينهم الإمام « الرازي » شارح الإشارات حيث يقول [ لما فرغ من بيان أن الجزئيات كيف تعلم حتى يلزم التغير ، وكيف تعلم حتى لا يلزم التغير ، وكان التغير على واجب الوجود ممتنعاً ، صرح في هذا الفصل بالنتيجة ، فقال يجب ألا يكون عالماً بالجزئيات علماً زمانيا متغيراً ، ويجب أن يكون عالماً بها على الوجه الثاني ، الذي لا يتغير بتغير الزمان ! . وأيضاً « الطوسى » حيث يقول ، تعليقاً على النص ، شارحاً ومعترضاً [ واعلم أن هذه السياقة ، تشبه سياقة الفقهاء ، في تخصيص بعض الأحكام العامة ، بأحكام تعارضها في الظاهر ، وذلك لأن الحكم بأن العلم بالعلة يوجب العلم بالمعلول ، إن لم يكن كليا ، لم يمكن أن يحكم بإحاطة الواجب بالكل ؛ وإن كان كليا ، وكان الجزئى المتغير ، من جملة معلولاته ، أوجب ذلك الحكم أن يكون عالماً به لا محالة فالقول بأنه لا يجوز أن يكون عالماً به ، لامتناع كون الواجب موضوعاً للتغير ، تخصيص لذلك الحكم الكلى بحكم آخر عارضه في بعض الصور ، وهذا دأب الفقهاء ، ومن يجرى مجراهم . ولا يجوز أمثال ذلك في المباحث المعقولة ، لامتناع تعارض الأحكام فيها ، فالصواب أن يؤخذ بيان يقع هذا المطلوب من مأخذ آخر ، وهو أن يقال ان : العلم بالعلة يوجب العلم بالمعلول ، ولا يوجب الإحساس به ، وإدراك الجزئيات المتغيرة ، من حيث هي متغيرة ، لا يمكن إلا بالآلات الجسمانية ، كالحواس وما يجرى مجراها ، والمدرك بذلك الإدراك يكون موضوعاً للتغير لا محالة ، أما إدراكها على الوجه الكلى ، فلا يمكن إلا أن يدرك بالعقل والمدرك بهذا الإدراك يمكن ألا يكون موضوعاً للتغير فإذن الواجب الأول ، وكل ما لا يكون موضوعاً للتغير ، بل كل ما هو عاقل ، يمتنع أن يدركها - من جهة ما هو عاقل - على الوجه الأول ، ويجب أن يدركها على الوجه الثاني ]
And you have known al-Ghazali's understanding of it, and many have agreed with him in this understanding; we mention among them Imam al-Razi, commentator on the Ishārāt, where he says: When he finished explaining how particulars are known such that change is necessary, and how they are known such that change is not necessary, and change is impossible for the Necessary Being, he stated the conclusion in this chapter, saying: He must not be knowledgeable of particulars with a changing temporal knowledge, and He must be knowledgeable of them in the second manner, which does not change with change of time. And also al-Tusi says, commenting on the text, explaining and objecting: Know that this way of presentation resembles the jurists' way in specifying general rulings with rulings that apparently contradict them. This is because the judgment that knowledge of the cause necessitates knowledge of the effect – if it is not universal, one cannot judge that the Necessary's encompassment of all [things] is necessary; and if it is universal, and the changing particular is among His effects, then that judgment must necessitate that He is knowledgeable of it inevitably. So the statement that He may not be knowledgeable of it, due to the impossibility of the Necessary being subject to change, is a specification of that universal judgment by another judgment that opposes it in some cases. And this is the practice of the jurists and those who follow their path. And such things are not permissible in rational inquiries, due to the impossibility of conflicting judgments in them. The correct approach is to take a proof that establishes this desired conclusion from another source, which is to say: Knowledge of the cause necessitates knowledge of the effect, but does not necessitate sensation of it. And perception of changing particulars, insofar as they are changing, is not possible except through bodily instruments like the senses and what resembles them; and the perceiver by that perception becomes subject to change inevitably. As for perceiving them in a universal manner, it is only possible to perceive them by the intellect, and the perceiver by this perception can be not subject to change. So then the First Necessary, and everything that is not subject to change – rather everything that is rational – it is impossible for it to perceive them – inasmuch as it is rational – in the first manner, and it must perceive them in the second manner.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage contains two commentaries. First, al-Razi explains that in the chapter, the author concluded that God cannot know particulars temporally because change is impossible for the Necessary Being; He must know them in the unchanging way. Second, al-Tusi notes that the reasoning resembles juristic argumentation: the general rule that knowledge of the cause gives knowledge of the effect would require God to know changing particulars, but the impossibility of change in God seems to specify that rule. Al-Tusi objects to this method as unsuitable for rational proofs because it introduces conflicting rulings. Instead, he offers an alternative: knowledge of the cause necessitates knowledge of the effect but not sensory perception; changing particulars are only perceived through bodily senses, but God, being immaterial and rational, perceives them universally without change. Thus, the Necessary Being perceives particulars in a universal, unchanging manner.

Key Terms
العلم بالعلة
المعلول
الإحساس
الآلات الجسمانية
الإدراك الكلى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that if God does not know particulars temporally, He does not know them at all.
Correction: The passage clarifies that God knows all particulars, including changing ones, but through a universal intellectual knowledge that does not involve sensory perception or change.

Checkpoint
According to al-Tusi's alternative argument, why can God know changing particulars without change in Himself?
A) Because He knows them through sensory instruments
B) Because He knows them universally by intellect, not sensorily
C) Because He only knows universals, not specifics
D) Because He changes along with the particulars

Key Takeaway
God knows all particulars, including changing ones, through a universal intellectual knowledge that does not require physical senses or entail change.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When do you rely more on your immediate sensory impressions than on a deeper, intellectual understanding? This lesson says God perceives wholly intellectually—are you willing to seek that same kind of universal clarity even when your emotions and senses pull you toward change?
?
Al-Tusi warns against specifying general rulings for convenience, calling it a juristic fallacy. Have you ever twisted a sound principle to justify your own exceptions? Will you now commit to truth that admits no convenient contradictions?
?
God knows every sin and shortcoming you have ever committed—and will ever commit—in a single, unchanging act of knowledge. If that does not unsettle you, it should; if it terrifies you, it humbles you. How will you live knowing that no particular flaw escapes His timeless awareness, yet that knowledge does not change Him—but it must change you?

Al-Shirazi's Rebuttal on God's Knowledge of Particulars
Al-Shirazi challenges al-Tusi's understanding and defends Ibn Sina's view of God's timeless knowledge of particulars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Shirazi's argument that God knows changing particulars without change through timeless knowledge expressed in nominal sentences.

Core Idea
God knows all changing particulars eternally, without change, using nominal sentences.

Al-Shirazi challenges the interpretation of al-Ghazali and al-Tusi, arguing that Ibn Sina's position is misread. He shows that God's knowledge of particulars, like Zayd's actions, does not imply change because God knows all times as present. This lesson unpacks his rebuttal and his key distinction between nominal and verbal sentences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage from al-Shirazi's rebuttal
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the flow of his argument from absurdity to solution.
2
Identify the absurd consequences
If God did not know individuals, absurdities follow: e.g., God would not know that Prophet Muhammad challenged with prophethood at that time.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Understand nominal vs. verbal sentences
Al-Shirazi says God knows particulars by nominal sentences (الجمل الاسمية) that describe states without tense, avoiding change.
Verbal sentences indicate a specific time, which would imply change.
4
Grasp the timelessness of God's knowledge
All times are present to God; there is no past, present, or future relative to His attributes.
5
Reflect on the denial of bodily instruments
Al-Shirazi denies that perception of changing particulars requires bodily instruments, saying it is only analogical to us.

Source Text
