فهو فهم « الغزالى » و « الرازی » و « الطوسي » فى النص ولكنه غير متعين فيه ، بل هناك ذاك لسواه ، « فالشيرازي » صاحب ( المحاكمات » يقول - نقلا عن حاشية « الشيخ محمد عبده » على العقائد العضدية ، ص ۱۱۲ - : { إن اعتراضه - يعنى الطوسي - وارد على فهمه هو من كلام الشيخ - يعنى « ابن سينا » – لا على مراد الشيخ ، كما حققناه ، لما أن العلم بالجزئيات المتغيرة ، أن زيداً مثلا ، لو أطاع الله تعالى أو عصاه ، لم يكن الله عز وجل عالماً بما يتجدد من أحواله ، لأنه لا يعرف زيداً بعينه ، فإنه شخص ، وأفعاله حادثة بعد أن لم تكن ، وإذا لم يعرف الشخص ، لم يعرف أحواله وأفعاله ، بل لا يعرف كفر زيد ولا إسلامه ، وإنما يعرف كفر الإنسان وإسلامه مطلقاً ، كلياً لا مخصوصاً بالأشخاص . بل يلزم أن يقال تحدى محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم – بالنبوة ، وهو لم يعرف في تلك الحال أنه تحدى به ، وكذلك الحال مع كل نبي معين ، وإنه إنما يعلم أن من الناس من يتحدى بالنبوة ، وأن صفة أولئك كذا وكذا ، فأما النبي المعين بشخصه ، فلا يعرفه ، فإن ذلك يعرف بالحس، والأحوال الصادرة · إنما يكون متغيراً ، لو كان ذلك العلم زمانيا ، أى مختصا بزمان، دون زمان ، ليتحقق العلم في زمان ، وعدمه في زمان آخر ، كما في علومنا وأما على الوجه المقدس عن الزمان، بأن يكون الواجب تعالى ، عالماً أزلا وأبداً ، بأن زيداً داخل في الدار في زمان كذا ، وخارج منها زمان كذا ، بعده أو قبله ، بالجمل الاسمية ، لا بالفعلية الدالة على أحد الأزمنة ؛ فلا تغير أصلا ، لأن جميع الأزمنة ، كجميع الأمكنة ، حاضرة عنده تعالى ، أزلا وأبداً ، فلا حال ولا حاضر ، ولا مستقبل ، بالنسبة إلى صفاته تعالى ، ولا بعيد ، من الأمكنة بالنسبة إليه تعالى • كما لا قريب وأما أن إدراك الجزئيات المتغيرة ، من حيث تغيرها ، لا يكون إلا بالآلات الجسمانية ، فمنوع بل إنما هو بالقياس إلينا أيضاً ] . وظاهر أن هذه نزعة أخرى تحاول أن تفهم النص على خلاف ما فهمه الغزالي وشيعته • ويعلق « الشيخ محمد عبده » على هذه المحاولة بقوله { وكلام الشيخ - يعنى « ابن سينا » – على هذا المحمل – يعنى محمل صاحب المحاكمات - من أحسن الكلام فى هذا الباب ، وهو تحقيق مذهب الفلاسفة وهذا الذي قد اشتهر عنهم ، شيء أخذ من ظاهر عباراتهم ، وجرى عليه بعض المتفلسفين ، جهلا ، فرجموا ظنا بغير علم . بل صريح عبارة الشيخ « أبى نصر الفارابي » في « الفصوص » أنه يعلم الجزئيات الشخصية على وجه شخصيتها ] هذان لوددان من الأفهام ، حاولا أن يفسرا النص ، ويشرحاه ، وهما متضاربان متباينان ، ولم أر بأساً في أن أطيل بذكرهما ، فإن الأمر جد خطير ، كما سنعرفه آخر الكتاب إن شاء الله
This is the understanding of al-Ghazali, al-Razi, and al-Tusi of the text, but it is not definite in it; rather there is another for another. Al-Shirazi, author of al-Muhakamat, says – quoting from the margin of Shaykh Muhammad Abduh on al-Aqaid al-Adudiyya, p. 112: His objection – meaning al-Tusi's – is directed at his own understanding of the master's speech – meaning Ibn Sina – not at the master's intention, as we have verified. For knowledge of changing particulars: if Zayd, for example, obeys God or disobeys Him, God would not know what occurs of His states, because He does not know Zayd himself, for he is a person, and his actions are contingent events that were not; and if He does not know the person, He does not know his states and actions. Rather He would not know Zayd's disbelief or Islam, but only knows disbelief and belief absolutely, universally, not assigned to persons. Rather it would be necessary to say that Muhammad – peace be upon him – challenged with prophethood, and He did not know at that time that he challenged with it, and similarly for every particular prophet. He only knows that among people there are those who challenge with prophethood, and their attributes are such and such. As for the particular prophet as a person, He does not know him, for that is known by sense and the states that occur. This would only be changed if that knowledge were temporal, i.e., specific to a time without a time, so that knowledge is realized in one time and absent in another time, as in our sciences. But on the face that is sacred from time, that the Necessary Exalted be knowing eternally and eternally that Zayd is inside the house at such a time and outside it at such a time, after or before, by nominal sentences, not by verbal ones indicating a tense; then there is no change at all, because all times, like all places, are present before Him Exalted, eternally and everlastingly. So there is no present, no past, no future relative to His attributes, nor near nor far of places relative to Him. As for the claim that perception of changing particulars, inasmuch as they are changing, is only through bodily instruments, it is denied; rather it is only by analogy to us as well. It is apparent that this is another tendency trying to understand the text contrary to what al-Ghazali and his followers understood. And Shaykh Muhammad Abduh comments on this attempt, saying: The master's – meaning Ibn Sina's – speech according to this interpretation – meaning the interpretation of the author of al-Muhakamat – is among the best speech on this topic, and it is a verification of the doctrine of the philosophers. And what has become famous about them is something taken from the apparent meaning of their statements, and some pseudo-philosophers have followed it out of ignorance and have stoned with conjecture without knowledge. Rather, the explicit statement of Shaykh Abu Nasr al-Farabi in al-Fusus is that He knows personal particulars in their particularity. These are two conflicting understandings that attempted to interpret the text and explain it, and they are contradictory and divergent. I saw no harm in lengthening the discussion with them, for the matter is very serious, as we will know at the end of the book, God willing.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage presents al-Shirazi's rebuttal to al-Tusi and al-Ghazali's interpretation, followed by Abduh's support for al-Shirazi. Al-Shirazi argues that if God did not know individual persons like Zayd, then He would not know Zayd's actions, faith, etc., which would absurdly imply that God did not know that Prophet Muhammad challenged with prophethood at that specific time. He insists that God's knowledge is eternal and all times are present to Him; the use of nominal sentences (describing states without tense) avoids change. He denies that perception of changing particulars requires bodily instruments, saying that is only an analogy from us. Thus he defends Ibn Sina's original intent as timeless knowledge that includes particulars. Abduh endorses this interpretation as the true philosophy, criticizing those who misrepresented the philosophers. The editor concludes by noting the two divergent interpretations and the gravity of the issue.

Key Terms
الجمل الاسمية
الجمل الفعلية
جزئيات متغيرة
الأزمنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's timeless knowledge cannot include changing particulars.
Correction: Al-Shirazi shows that timeless knowledge via nominal sentences includes all particulars without change.

Checkpoint
According to al-Shirazi, how does God know changing particulars without change?
A) By using bodily instruments
B) By knowing only universals
C) By knowing them eternally with nominal sentences that do not indicate tense
D) By relying on sensory perception

Key Takeaway
Al-Shirazi argues that God's knowledge of particulars is timeless and unchanging, expressed through nominal sentences, avoiding the pitfalls of temporal knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that God knows every detail of your life – every sin, every hidden thought – eternally and unchangingly, why do you still act as if He does not see? How will you live today knowing that your every action is before Him?
?
Al-Shirazi says God knows your obedience or disobedience before you commit it. Does this give you a sense of predestination that paralyses you, or does it motivate you to strive harder knowing that God's knowledge does not remove your responsibility? Search your heart.
?
You often pray to God as if He is distant in time. But this lesson shows that all times are present to Him. Tonight, in your dua, speak to the Eternal One who knows your past, present, and future as one. What will you say differently?

Abduh's Endorsement of al-Shirazi's Interpretation
Abduh supports al-Shirazi's reading and warns against misrepresenting the philosophers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abduh's support for al-Shirazi's interpretation and his critique of pseudo-philosophers, as well as the editor's warning about the gravity of the issue.

Core Idea
Abduh affirms that Ibn Sina's doctrine, properly understood, is that God knows personal particulars.

After al-Shirazi's rebuttal, Shaykh Muhammad Abduh comments, praising al-Shirazi's interpretation as the true philosophy. He criticizes those who misrepresented the philosophers. The editor concludes by noting the serious divergence of opinions. This lesson examines these reflections.

Guided Path
1
Read Abduh's comment and the editor's conclusion
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the shift from al-Shirazi's argument to Abduh's assessment and the editor's note.
2
Understand why Abduh calls it 'the best speech'
Abduh says al-Shirazi's interpretation is among the best on this topic and a verification of the philosophers' doctrine.
3
Note Abduh's criticism of pseudo-philosophers
Abduh warns that some have misrepresented the philosophers by taking apparent meanings, leading to ignorance and false accusations.
He cites al-Farabi as explicitly stating God knows personal particulars.
4
Reflect on the editor's statement
The editor notes the two contradictory interpretations and the seriousness of the matter, promising further discussion at the end of the book.

Divine Attributes
intermediate
6 min
God's Knowledge of Particulars is Eternal and Unchanging
Ghazali refutes the claim that God cannot know changing particulars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that God's knowledge of changing particulars does not change because it is timeless and encompasses all times simultaneously.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is eternal and timeless, so changing events do not change His knowledge.

Ghazali presents a common philosophical objection that God cannot know changing particulars, then refutes it by clarifying the nature of divine knowledge. He shows that the objection mistakenly assumes God's knowledge is like human temporal knowledge. This passage unpacks the distinction between temporal and eternal knowledge, using the example of Zayd and the Prophet Muhammad.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing, then read the English translation. Try to grasp the overall flow: the objection first, then Ghazali's reply.
Notice the shift in tone when Ghazali begins his refutation with 'but on the holy manner...'
2
Identify the philosophical objection
The first part of the passage (up to 'as in our knowledges') presents an argument from the philosophers. Find the key claim about Zayd and Muhammad, and why the philosophers think divine knowledge would be deficient.
The objection hinges on the assumption that knowledge of particulars requires temporal change.
3
Understand Ghazali's counter-argument
Ghazali responds by distinguishing temporal knowledge from eternal knowledge. He uses the concept of 'nominal sentences' versus 'verbal sentences' to explain how God's knowledge is timeless.
Think of nominal sentences as stating facts without tense, e.g., 'Zayd is inside at time T' rather than 'Zayd was inside'.
4
Reflect on the denial of bodily instruments
In the final sentence, Ghazali rejects the claim that perception of changing particulars requires bodily organs. This rebuts a separate philosophical assumption.
5
Test your understanding with the checkpoint
Answer the multiple-choice question to check your grasp of the core argument.
The correct answer will be directly supported by the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن زيداً مثلا ، لو أطاع الله تعالى أو عصاه ، لم يكن الله عز وجل عالماً بما يتجدد من أحواله ، لأنه لا يعرف زيداً بعينه ، فإنه شخص ، وأفعاله حادثة بعد أن لم تكن ، وإذا لم يعرف الشخص ، لم يعرف أحواله وأفعاله ، بل لا يعرف كفر زيد ولا إسلامه ، وإنما يعرف كفر الإنسان وإسلامه مطلقاً ، كلياً لا مخصوصاً بالأشخاص . بل يلزم أن يقال تحدى محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم – بالنبوة ، وهو لم يعرف في تلك الحال أنه تحدى به ، وكذلك الحال مع كل نبي معين ، وإنه إنما يعلم أن من الناس من يتحدى بالنبوة ، وأن صفة أولئك كذا وكذا ، فأما النبي المعين بشخصه ، فلا يعرفه ، فإن ذلك يعرف بالحس، والأحوال الصادرة · إنما يكون متغيراً ، لو كان ذلك العلم زمانيا ، أى مختصا بزمان، دون زمان ، ليتحقق العلم في زمان ، وعدمه في زمان آخر ، كما في علومنا وأما على الوجه المقدس عن الزمان، بأن يكون الواجب تعالى ، عالماً أزلا وأبداً ، بأن زيداً داخل في الدار في زمان كذا ، وخارج منها زمان كذا ، بعده أو قبله ، بالجمل الاسمية ، لا بالفعلية الدالة على أحد الأزمنة ؛ فلا تغير أصلا ، لأن جميع الأزمنة ، كجميع الأمكنة ، حاضرة عنده تعالى ، أزلا وأبداً ، فلا حال ولا حاضر ، ولا مستقبل ، بالنسبة إلى صفاته تعالى ، ولا بعيد ، من الأمكنة بالنسبة إليه تعالى • كما لا قريب وأما أن إدراك الجزئيات المتغيرة ، من حيث تغيرها ، لا يكون إلا بالآلات الجسمانية ، فمنوع بل إنما هو بالقياس إلينا أيضاً ]
That Zayd, for example, if he obeys God or disobeys Him, God would not be knowing what renews of his states, because He does not know Zayd specifically, for he is a person, and his actions are originated after they were not. And if He does not know the person, He does not know his states and actions. Rather, He does not know Zayd's disbelief nor his Islam, but He knows disbelief and Islam absolutely, universally, not specifically to persons. Rather, it follows that it be said that Muhammad (peace be upon him) challenged with prophethood, and He did not know at that time that he challenged with it, and likewise the case with every specific prophet. And He only knows that among people are those who challenge with prophethood, and that the description of those is such-and-such. As for the specific prophet in his person, He does not know him, for that is known by sense and the emitted states. It would only be changing if that knowledge were temporal, i.e., specific to a time without another time, so that knowledge would be realized at one time and absent at another, as in our knowledges. But on the holy manner that is free from time, namely that the Necessary (most high) is knowing eternally and forever that Zayd is inside the house at such a time, and outside it at such a time, after it or before it, by nominal sentences, not by verbal ones indicating one of the tenses; so there is no change at all, because all times, like all places, are present to Him (most high), eternally and forever. So there is no past, present, or future with respect to His attributes, and no near or far place with respect to Him. As for the perception of changing particulars, from the aspect of their change, not being except by bodily instruments, it is denied; rather it is only by analogy to us also.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from the philosophers that God cannot know changing particulars, and Ghazali's refutation. The passage first sets up the objection: if Zayd obeys or disobeys, God would not know his changing states because He does not know Zayd as a particular person. It then extends this to prophets: God would not know that Muhammad specifically challenged with prophethood. Then Ghazali responds by distinguishing temporal knowledge from eternal knowledge. If knowledge were temporal, it would change. But God's knowledge is eternal and encompasses all times simultaneously. He knows events by nominal sentences, not verbal ones that indicate tense, so no change occurs. He then adds that the claim that perception of changing particulars requires bodily instruments is denied; it is only true by analogy to us.

Key Terms
جزئيات متغيرة
علم أزلي
جمل اسمية
تغير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's knowledge is like human knowledge and must change with time.
Correction: God's knowledge is eternal and timeless; it does not change because it encompasses all times simultaneously.

Checkpoint
What reason does Ghazali give for God's knowledge not changing?
A) God does not know particulars at all.
B) God's knowledge is temporal.
C) God's knowledge is eternal, knowing events by nominal sentences.
D) God uses bodily instruments to perceive change.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge of changing particulars is unchanging because it is eternal and knows events by nominal sentences that do not indicate tense.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The lesson shows that God sees all your actions—past, present, and future—as present to Him. Is there a sin you are hiding or delaying repentance from, thinking it is hidden or that you have time? If God already knows it eternally, what prevents you from turning to Him now?
?
If God's knowledge never changes and He knows every failure of yours before it happens, does that make you feel hopeless or accountable? How does the fact that He knows everything eternally, yet still sustains you, challenge your view of His mercy?
?
When you commit a sin, you experience the moment as new and changing. But God's knowledge of that sin was already there from eternity. How does that timeless perspective shift your urgency to purify your heart, knowing that every inclination is already seen?

Three Kinds of Change and the Impossibility of Change in God
Ghazali classifies three kinds of change and shows the philosophers' view implies change in essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's classification of three kinds of change and why change in essence cannot be attributed to God.

Core Idea
Change in essence is impossible for God, but relational change is not.

In his refutation of the philosophers, Ghazali identifies three distinct kinds of change: pure relational change, change due to removal of objects of power, and change in essence. He argues that the philosophers' view that God's knowledge changes with particulars would require the third kind, which is impossible for the unchanging Divine Essence. This passage lays out that classification.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get a feel for Ghazali's argument. Then read the English translation to ensure understanding.
2
Identify the three kinds of change
Ghazali enumerates three kinds of change: pure relational, change in relation due to objects, and change in essence. Note the examples for each.
The first two are relational and do not affect the essence.
3
Understand the philosophers' error
Ghazali uses this classification to show that the philosophers' position (that God's knowledge changes) would actually be a change in essence, which is impossible for God.
4
Reflect on the theological significance
Consider why this distinction matters for divine transcendence and immutability.
This connects to the broader theme of God's attributes being eternal and unchanging.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و خبالهم أن هذه أحوال ثلاثة مختلفة ، والمختلفات إذا تعاقبت على محل واحد أوجبت فيه تغيراً ، لا محالة ، فإن كان في حال الكسوف ، عالماً بأنه كما كان قبله ، فهو جاهل لا عالم ، وإن كان عالماً بأنه كائن سيكون وقبل ذلك كان عالماً ، بأنه ليس بكائن وأنه سيكون ، فقد اختلف علمه ، واختلف حاله ، فلزم التغير ، إذ لا معنى للتغير ، إلا اختلاف العالم ، فإن من لم يعلم شيئاً ثم علمه ، فقد تغير ، ومن لم يكن له علم بأنه كائن ، ثم حصل حال الوجود فقد تغير وحققوا هذا ، بأن الأحوال ثلاثة : حال إضافة محضة ، ككونك يميناً وشمالاً ، فإن هذا لا يرجع إلى هي وصف ذاتى ، بل هو إضافة محضة ، فإن تحول الشيء الذي كان على يمينك إلى شمالك ، تغيرت إضافتك ، ولم تتغير ذاتك بحال ، وهذا تبدل إضافة على الذات ، وليس بتبدل الذات (1) هذا القبيل إذا كنت قادراً على تحريك أجسام حاضرة بين يديك، ومن فانعدمت الأجسام أو انعدم بعضها ، لم تتغير قواك الغريزية ، ولا قدرتك ― ، لأن قدرتك قدرة على تحريك الجسم المطلق أولا ، ثم على المعين ثانياً ـ من حيث إنه جسم - فلم تكن إضافة القدرة إلى الجسم المعين ، وصفاً ذاتياً، بل إضافة محضة ، فتغيرها يوجب زوال إضافة ، لا تغيراً فى حال القادر . والثالث تغير في الذات ، وهو ألا يكون عالماً ، فيعلم ؛ ألا يكون قادراً ، فيقدر ، فهذا تغير (1) لعل هذا هو الحال الثانية . والحال الثالثة تأتى بعد .
And their confusion is that these are three different states, and when different things succeed upon a single subject, they necessitate change in it, unavoidably. For if at the time of eclipse he is knowing that it is as it was before, then he is ignorant, not knowing. And if he is knowing that it is going to be and before that he was knowing that it is not going to be and that it will be, then his knowledge has differed and his state has differed, so change is necessary, for there is no meaning of change except difference of the knower. For whoever did not know something and then knew it, has changed. And whoever did not have knowledge that it is, then the state of existence occurred, has changed. And they verified this: that the states are three: a state of pure relation, like you being to the right and left. For this does not go back to a description pertaining to the essence, but rather it is a pure relation. For if the thing that was on your right turns to your left, your relation changes but your essence does not change at all. This is a change of relation upon the essence, not a change of the essence. (1) This kind is when you are able to move bodies present before you, and if the bodies cease to exist or some of them cease, your innate powers and your ability do not change, because your ability is an ability to move the absolute body first, then to the specific one second, insofar as it is a body. So the relation of ability to the specific body was not an essential description, but a pure relation, so its change necessitates the removal of a relation, not a change in the state of the capable one. And the third is change in the essence, which is that one is not knowing, then knows; or is not able, then becomes able. So this is change. (1) Perhaps this is the second state, and the third state comes after.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage is a classification of three kinds of change, used by Ghazali to show that the philosophers' position would require a change in God's essence, which is impossible. Ghazali begins by stating that the philosophers' confusion lies in three different states that when they succeed upon a subject cause change. He gives an example of an eclipse: if the knower's knowledge differs, change occurs. Then he enumerates the three kinds of change: (1) pure relational change, like left/right, which does not affect the essence; (2) change in relation due to removal of objects of power, where the power itself remains unchanged; and (3) change in essence, such as acquiring knowledge or power. The philosophers' claim that God's knowledge changes with particulars would necessitate the third kind, which is impossible for God. Hence their position is refuted.

Key Terms
أحوال ثلاثة
إضافة محضة
تغير في الذات
قدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That any change in God's knowledge is a change in relation, not in essence.
Correction: Ghazali argues that the philosophers' view implies change in essence (third kind), not merely relational change, because God's knowledge would differ from one time to another, which is a change in the attribute of knowledge itself.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, which kind of change is impossible for God?
A) Change in pure relation
B) Change in relation due to removal of objects
C) Change in essence
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's threefold classification of change shows that only change in essence is impossible for God, thereby refuting the philosophers' claim that God's knowledge changes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you sometimes imagine that God's knowledge of your life changes as your circumstances do, as if He is surprised by your choices? How does this lesson's distinction between relational and essential change challenge that assumption, and what does it mean for your trust in His sovereignty?
?
If God's essence never changes, then His love, mercy, and justice are constant. Is there an area of your life where you act as if you need to earn a different response from Him—as if He could change from displeased to pleased? How does Ghazali's classification expose that as a misunderstanding of God's unchanging essence?

Classifying Change: Relational vs. Essential
Ghazali classifies the first two types of change as relational.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three-fold classification of change in the kalam tradition and why only one type constitutes real change in the essence.

Core Idea
Not all change is equal; only essential change affects the substance.

Ghazali begins his refutation by presenting the philosophers' claim that there are three types of change. He carefully distinguishes between changes that are merely relational and those that affect the essence itself. Understanding this classification is crucial before we can evaluate whether any change can be attributed to God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage identifying the three states
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice how the philosophers present the three states.
The passage starts with 'وخبالهم' and introduces the three states.
2
Understand the first type: pure relation
The first type is pure relational change, like an object moving from your right to your left. Note that your essence does not change, only the relation.
This is an external relation, not an essential attribute.
3
Understand the second type: change in relation of ability
The second type involves ability: if objects you can move cease to exist, your ability does not change essentially; only the relation to specific objects changes.
This is also a pure relation, not essential change.
4
Anticipate the third type
The third type (change in essence) will be covered in the next lesson. For now, note that Ghazali has set up a clear distinction between relational and essential change.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخبالهم أن هذه أحوال ثلاثة مختلفة، والمختلفات إذا تعاقبت على محل واحد أوجبت فيه تغيراً، لا محالة، فإن كان في حال الكسوف، عالماً بأنه كما كان قبله، فهو جاهل لا عالم، وإن كان عالماً بأنه كائن سيكون وقبل ذلك كان عالماً، بأنه ليس بكائن وأنه سيكون، فقد اختلف علمه، واختلف حاله، فلزم التغير، إذ لا معنى للتغير، إلا اختلاف العالم، فإن من لم يعلم شيئاً ثم علمه، فقد تغير، ومن لم يكن له علم بأنه كائن، ثم حصل حال الوجود فقد تغير وحققوا هذا، بأن الأحوال ثلاثة : حال إضافة محضة، ككونك يميناً وشمالاً، فإن هذا لا يرجع إلى هي وصف ذاتى، بل هو إضافة محضة، فإن تحول الشيء الذي كان على يمينك إلى شمالك، تغيرت إضافتك، ولم تتغير ذاتك بحال، وهذا تبدل إضافة على الذات، وليس بتبدل الذات هذا القبيل إذا كنت قادراً على تحريك أجسام حاضرة بين يديك، ومن فانعدمت الأجسام أو انعدم بعضها، لم تتغير قواك الغريزية، ولا قدرتك ـ لأن قدرتك قدرة على تحريك الجسم المطلق أولا، ثم على المعين ثانياً ـ من حيث إنه جسم فلم تكن إضافة القدرة إلى الجسم المعين، وصفاً ذاتياً، بل إضافة محضة، فتغيرها يوجب زوال إضافة، لا تغيراً فى حال القادر . والثالث، تغير في الذات، وهو ألا يكون عالماً، فيعلم ؛ أولا يكون قادراً، فيقدر، فهذا تغير وتغير المعلوم يوجب تغير العلم، فإن حقيقة ذات العلم، تدخل فيه الإضافة إلى المعلوم الخاص، إذ حقيقة العلم المعين، تعلقه بذلك المعلوم المعين، على ما هو عليه، فتعلقه به على وجه آخر، علم آخر بالضرورة، فتعاقبهما اختلاف حال العالم. يوجب ولا يمكن أن يقال : إن للذات علماً واحداً، فيصير علماً بالكون، بعد كونه علماً بأنه سيكون، ثم هو يصير علماً بأنه كان، بعد أن كان علماً بعد بأنه كائن، فالعلم واحد متشابه الأحوال، وقد تبدلت عليه الإضافة، لأن الإضافة فى العلم حقيقة ذات العلم، فتبدلها يوجب تبدل ذات العلم، فيلزمه التغير، وهو محال على الله تعالى
And their folly is that these are three different states, and when different things succeed one another in a single locus, they necessarily cause change in it. For if during the eclipse he is knowing that it is as it was before, then he is ignorant, not knowing. And if he is knowing that it is going to be, and before that he was knowing that it is not going to be and that it will be, then his knowledge has differed and his state has differed, so change is necessary, for the meaning of change is nothing but the difference of the knower. For one who did not know something and then knew it has changed, and one who had no knowledge that it is and then the state of existence occurred has changed. And they verified this by [saying] that the states are three: a state of pure relation, like your being on the right and left, for this does not refer to an essential attribute, but is a pure relation. For if the thing that was on your right moves to your left, your relation changes, and your essence does not change at all. This is a substitution of relation upon the essence, not a substitution of the essence. And this category [includes] if you are able to move bodies present before you, and if the bodies cease to exist or some of them cease, your innate powers and your ability do not change, because your ability is an ability to move the absolute body first, then the specific body second, insofar as it is a body. So the relation of ability to the specific body was not an essential attribute, but a pure relation; its change necessitates the removal of a relation, not a change in the state of the capable. And the third [type] is change in the essence, which is that he is not knowing and then knows, or is not able and then is able; this is change. And change of the known necessitates change of knowledge, for the reality of the essence of knowledge includes within it the relation to the specific known, since the reality of a particular knowledge is its connection to that particular known as it is. So its connection to it in another way is necessarily another knowledge, and their succession is a difference in the state of the knower. And it cannot be said that the essence has one knowledge, so it becomes knowledge of existence after having been knowledge that it will be, then it becomes knowledge that it was after having been knowledge that it is, for the knowledge is one, similar in states, and the relation has been substituted upon it, because relation in knowledge is the reality of the essence of knowledge, so its substitution necessitates substitution of the essence of knowledge, necessitating change, which is impossible for God Almighty.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' claim that God's knowledge of particulars would change. Ghazali presents the philosophers' argument (their 'folly') that three types of change exist, and then demonstrates that only one type constitutes true change in the knower, which cannot apply to God. The passage begins by stating that different states succeeding one another cause change in a locus. Applied to knowledge: if during an eclipse God knows it as it was before, He is ignorant; if He knows it will be and then knows it is, His knowledge differs, implying change. The philosophers reduced change to three types: (1) Pure relational change, like an object moving from your right to your left—your essence does not change, only the relation. (2) Change in the relation of ability—if objects you can move cease to exist, your power does not change essentially, only the relation to specific objects vanishes. (3) Essential change—going from not knowing to knowing, or from not having power to having power. This is real change in the essence. Then Ghazali applies this to divine knowledge: a change in the known object necessitates a change in knowledge, because knowledge's essence includes its relation to the specific known object. If the relation changes, the knowledge itself changes, leading to essential change in the knower. He rejects the idea that one knowledge can relate to different states (future, present, past) as mere relations, because the relation is integral to the knowledge's essence. Thus, such change is absolutely impossible for God.

Key Terms
أحوال
إضافة محضة
تغير الذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any change in relation or ability implies a change in the essence of the subject.
Correction: Ghazali shows that pure relational changes (like an object moving from right to left) and changes in the relation of ability (like objects ceasing to exist) do not affect the essence of the subject; only changes from not knowing to knowing or not able to able constitute essential change.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, which of the following is an example of pure relational change?
A) A person moving from right to left
B) A person gaining knowledge of a new fact
C) A person acquiring the ability to lift a weight
D) A person forgetting a past event

Key Takeaway
Only changes that affect the essence are real changes; relational changes do not alter the subject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When your abilities change (like losing a job or skill), do you feel your identity is threatened? How does this lesson challenge that feeling? Are you willing to see those as mere relational changes?
?
Consider a time when a relationship shifted (someone moved away or changed). Did you feel that your essence changed? What would it look like to believe that your essence remains unchanged despite such shifts?
?
If you truly believed that only essential changes matter, what would you stop worrying about today? What would you start focusing on instead?

Why God's Knowledge Cannot Change
Ghazali argues that the third type of change cannot apply to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a change in the known object would necessitate a change in God's knowledge, making God's essence subject to change, which is impossible.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is not merely related to its objects; it is defined by them.

Having clarified the types of change, Ghazali now applies this to divine knowledge. He argues that if God's knowledge of an event changes from future to present to past, the knowledge itself would change, implying a change in God's essence. This is impossible for the immutable God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the third type of change
Read the Arabic passage focusing on the third type of change: change in the essence. Then read the translation carefully.
This type is defined as going from not knowing to knowing, or from not able to able.
2
Understand the argument: change in known necessitates change in knowledge
Ghazali argues that because the essence of knowledge includes the relation to the specific known, a change in the known object (e.g., from future to present) changes the knowledge itself.
This is not a mere relational change; it is essential.
3
Consider the counterargument and its refutation
The philosophers might say that one knowledge can have different relations over time. Ghazali refutes this by stating that in knowledge, the relation is part of the essence, so changing the relation changes the knowledge.
This is the key point distinguishing divine knowledge from human relational changes.
4
Conclude: change is impossible for God
Since any change in God's knowledge would entail an essential change, and God is immutable, such change is absolutely impossible.

Source Text

So the relation of ability to the specific body was not an essential attribute, but a pure relation; its change necessitates the removal of a relation, not a change in the state of the capable. And the third [type] is change in the essence, which is that he is not knowing and then knows, or is not able and then is able; this is change. And change of the known necessitates change of knowledge, for the reality of the essence of knowledge includes within it the relation to the specific known, since the reality of a particular knowledge is its connection to that particular known as it is. So its connection to it in another way is necessarily another knowledge, and their succession is a difference in the state of the knower. And it cannot be said that the essence has one knowledge, so it becomes knowledge of existence after having been knowledge that it will be, then it becomes knowledge that it was after having been knowledge that it is, for the knowledge is one, similar in states, and the relation has been substituted upon it, because relation in knowledge is the reality of the essence of knowledge, so its substitution necessitates substitution of the essence of knowledge, necessitating change, which is impossible for God Almighty.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' claim that God's knowledge of particulars would change. Ghazali presents the philosophers' argument (their 'folly') that three types of change exist, and then demonstrates that only one type constitutes true change in the knower, which cannot apply to God. The passage begins by stating that different states succeeding one another cause change in a locus. Applied to knowledge: if during an eclipse God knows it as it was before, He is ignorant; if He knows it will be and then knows it is, His knowledge differs, implying change. The philosophers reduced change to three types: (1) Pure relational change, like an object moving from your right to your left—your essence does not change, only the relation. (2) Change in the relation of ability—if objects you can move cease to exist, your power does not change essentially, only the relation to specific objects vanishes. (3) Essential change—going from not knowing to knowing, or from not having power to having power. This is real change in the essence. Then Ghazali applies this to divine knowledge: a change in the known object necessitates a change in knowledge, because knowledge's essence includes its relation to the specific known object. If the relation changes, the knowledge itself changes, leading to essential change in the knower. He rejects the idea that one knowledge can relate to different states (future, present, past) as mere relations, because the relation is integral to the knowledge's essence. Thus, such change is absolutely impossible for God.

Key Terms
تغير الذات
علم
إضافة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's knowledge could remain numerically one while its relation to different temporal states changes, similar to how a person's relation to an object changes without changing the person.
Correction: Ghazali argues that in the case of knowledge, the relation to the known is not external but constitutive of the knowledge's essence; thus a change in relation necessitates a change in the knowledge itself, implying an essential change in the knower, which is impossible for God.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, which type of change is considered real change in the essence?
A) Pure relational change
B) Change in the relation of ability
C) Change from not knowing to knowing
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
For God, any change in the known object would necessitate a change in His knowledge, which implies a change in His essence, and that is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Are you living as if God already knows every outcome? What would change in your daily dua and tawakkul if you internalized this?
?
Ghazali argues that any change in God’s knowledge would imply a change in His essence. Does your heart accept that God is absolutely perfect and unchanging, or do you sometimes wish He would change His decree for you? What does that reveal about your understanding of His attributes?
?
The passage says that knowledge is defined by its relation to the known. When you think about God knowing your sin before you committed it, does that make you feel hopeless or accountable? How should this truth affect your repentance and your trust in His mercy?

Objection: One Knowledge Does Not Change
Ghazali presents an objection that God's one knowledge relates to temporal events as mere additions, negating real change.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection argues that God's one knowledge can relate to future, present, and past events through mere relational additions without any change in His essence.

Core Idea
God's one knowledge remains the same; only its relation to events changes.

This lesson presents a key objection against the idea that God's knowledge must change with temporal events. The objector uses the example of an eclipse to show that God's single knowledge can relate to future, present, and past without any change in itself. Before diving into the passage, consider how your own knowledge of a future event, when it occurs, remains the same but its relation to time changes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the full Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: the objector states the position, gives the eclipse example, and explains the analogy of the moving person.
Pay special attention to the phrase 'ترجع إلى إضافات'—'revert to relations'.
2
Identify the core argument
The objector claims that differences in God's knowledge over time are merely relational additions (iḍāfāt) that do not affect the essence of knowledge. Find the sentence that says 'these differences revert to relations' and the conclusion that they do not necessitate change in the knower.
3
Analyze the analogy
The analogy: a single person moves from your right to your front to your left. Your relation to him changes, but you do not change. How does this analogy support the claim about God's knowledge? Relate it to the eclipse example.
The moving person represents the event; the fixed observer represents God.
4
Connect to the agreed goal
The objector ends by noting that both sides agree on negating change in God. Why is this agreement important? How does the objection use it to support its own position?
This shows the objection is not introducing a novel idea but defending a shared principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والاعتراض من وجهين : أحدهما أن يقال : ثم تنكرون على من يقول : إن الله تعالى، له علم واحد، بوجود الكسوف مثلا، في وقت معين، وذلك العلم قبل وجوده، علم بأنه سيكون، وهو بعينه عند الوجود، علم بأنه كائن، وهو بعينه بعد الانجلاء، علم بالانقضاء، وأن هذه الاختلافات، ترجع إلى إضافات، لا توجب تبدلا في ذات العلم، فلا توجب تغيراً فى ذات العالم، فإن ذلك ينزل منزلة الإضافة المحضة، فإن الشخص الواحد، يكون عن يمينك، ثم يرجع إلى قدامك، ثم إلى شمالك، فتتعاقب عليك الإضافات، والمتغير ذلك الشخص المنتقل، دونك وهكذا ينبغي أن يفهم الحال في علم الله عز وجل، فإنا نسلم أنه يعلم الأشياء بعلم واحد، في الأزل والأبد والحال، لا يتغير وغرضهم نفى التغير، وهو متفق عليه
And the objection is from two aspects: One of them is that it may be said: Then you deny those who say that God Almighty has one knowledge of the existence of the eclipse, for example, at a specific time; that knowledge before its existence is knowledge that it will be, and the very same at the time of existence is knowledge that it is, and the very same after the clearing is knowledge of its passing; and that these differences revert to relations, which do not necessitate a change in the essence of knowledge, and thus do not necessitate a change in the essence of the knower. For this is analogous to pure relation: a single person can be to your right, then moves to your front, then to your left, so relations succeed upon you, and the changing entity is that moving person, not you. Likewise should the situation be understood regarding the knowledge of God Almighty; for we concede that He knows things with one knowledge, in pre-eternity and post-eternity and the present, not changing. And their purpose is to negate change, and that is agreed upon.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against the previous argument that God's knowledge would change. The objector argues from two aspects; here only the first is given. The objection states that God has one single knowledge of a particular event like an eclipse; before the event, that knowledge is knowledge that it will be; during the eclipse, it is knowledge that it is; after, knowledge that it has passed. These differences are merely relational additions (iḍāfāt) that do not change the essence of the knowledge itself, just as a person moving from your right to your left changes your relation to him, but you do not change. Thus, God's one knowledge remains unchanged, and its relations to different times do not imply change in God. The objector concludes that this is the correct understanding of divine knowledge, and that the goal of all parties is to negate change in God, which is agreed upon.

Key Terms
اعتراض (objection)
إضافة (relation)
علم واحد (one knowledge)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A change in the known object necessarily causes a change in the knower's essence.
Correction: The objection clarifies that relational changes do not affect the essence; the knower's knowledge remains one and unchanged, just as your position relative to a moving person changes without you changing.

Checkpoint

A) It changes essentially with each state
B) It remains the same but its relation to the event differs
C) It becomes a new knowledge for each state
D) It is limited to the present only

Key Takeaway
God's single, eternal knowledge relates to temporal events through unchanging essence and varied relations, preserving divine immutability.

Change in the Known Necessitates Change in Knowledge
Ghazali argues that change in the known necessitates change in knowledge, impossible for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that any change in the object of knowledge forces a change in the knowledge itself, and why this is impossible for God.

Core Idea
A change in what is known forces a change in the knowledge itself — but God's knowledge is unchanging.

This passage presents a key logical premise in Ghazali's critique of the philosophers: knowledge is essentially relational, so a change in the object necessarily changes the knowledge. Ghazali then rejects the objection that a single knowledge could cover changing states, concluding that such change is impossible for God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the tight logical flow: change in known → change in knowledge → change in knower.
Pay attention to the term 'إضافة' (relation) — it is central.
2
Identify the key premise
The passage states that the relation to the particular known is part of the essence of knowledge. Why does this make change inevitable when the object changes?
3
Follow the objection and rebuttal
The philosophers might say knowledge remains one while relations change. Ghazali rebuts that relation is intrinsic to the essence, so changing it changes the knowledge itself.
This is the critical move in the argument.
4
Understand the conclusion
Since change in knowledge would imply change in the knower, and God cannot change, God's knowledge must be unchanging. Ask yourself: does this argument rule out all change in divine knowledge?
Reflect on the implications for divine omniscience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتغير المعلوم يوجب تغير العلم ، فإن حقيقة ذات العلم ، تدخل فيه الإضافة إلى المعلوم الخاص ، إذ حقيقة العلم المعين ، تعلقه بذلك المعلوم المعين ، على ما هو عليه ، فتعلقه به على وجه آخر ، علم آخر بالضرورة ، فتعاقبهما اختلاف حال العالم . ولا يمكن أن يقال : إن للذات علماً واحداً ، فيصير علماً بالكون ، بعد كونه علماً بأنه سيكون ، ثم هو يصير علماً بأنه كان ، بعد أن كان علماً [؟] بأنه كائن ، فالعلم واحد متشابه الأحوال ، وقد تبدلت عليه الإضافة ، لأن الإضافة فى العلم حقيقة ذات العلم ، فتبدلها يوجب تبدل ذات العلم ، فيلزمه التغير ، وهو محال على الله تعالى
And change of the known necessitates change of knowledge, for the reality of the essence of knowledge includes the relation to the particular known, since the reality of a specific knowledge is its connection to that specific known as it is. So its connection to it in another aspect is necessarily another knowledge. So their succession is a difference in the state of the knower. And it cannot be said: that the essence has one knowledge, which becomes knowledge of existence after being knowledge that it will be, then becomes knowledge that it was after being knowledge [؟] that it is, so that knowledge is one with similar states, and the relation has changed upon it, because relation in knowledge is the reality of the essence of knowledge, so its change necessitates change of the essence of knowledge, which entails change, and that is impossible for God, the Exalted.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage is a logical proof that change in the object of knowledge necessitates change in the knowledge itself, because the essence of a particular knowledge is constituted by its relation to its object. Therefore, if the object changes (e.g., from future to present to past), the knowledge must change accordingly. The passage then rejects the possibility that a single knowledge could cover different states merely by changes in relation, since relation is intrinsic to knowledge's essence. Such change in knowledge would imply change in the knower, which is impossible for God. Thus, the argument establishes that God's knowledge must be unchanging.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a single piece of knowledge can remain the same while its object changes (e.g., knowing a thing as future, then as present, then as past).
Correction: Ghazali argues that knowledge is defined by its relation to the object; changing the relation changes the knowledge itself. Therefore, the same knowledge cannot encompass different temporal states.

Key Takeaway

Objection: One Knowledge Covers All States via Relations
Ghazali presents the philosophers' objection that knowledge differences are merely relational.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that God's single knowledge relates to different states without change, using the example of an eclipse and the analogy of a moving person.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is one, its apparent differences are only relational.

In this passage, Ghazali presents the first philosophical objection to the claim that God's knowledge changes with its objects. The philosophers argue that a single knowledge can relate to past, present, and future without any intrinsic change. They illustrate this with the example of an eclipse and a spatial analogy. Understanding this objection is key to the subsequent refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice that Ghazali prefaces it as the first of two objections.
The objection is presented in a compact argument.
2
Examine the eclipse example
Identify how the philosophers use the example of an eclipse to show that one knowledge can relate to three temporal states (future, present, past) without changing.
The example is central to their claim.
3
Analyze the spatial analogy
Study the analogy of a person moving around you. Understand how the relations change but the knower (you) remains unchanged.
This clarifies that change is in the relation, not the essence.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The philosophers conclude that God's knowledge is one and unchanging, and differences are merely relational. Their purpose is to deny change in God, which is agreed upon.
This sets up the debate for Ghazali's response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والاعتراض من وجهين : أحدهما أن يقال : ثم تنكرون على من يقول : إن الله تعالى له علم واحد بوجود الكسوف مثلا في وقت معين وذلك العلم قبل وجوده علم بأنه سيكون وهو بعينه عند الوجود علم بأنه كائن وهو بعينه بعد الانجلاء علم بالانقضاء وأن هذه الاختلافات ترجع إلى إضافات لا توجب تبدلا في ذات العلم فلا توجب تغيراً في ذات العالم فإن ذلك ينزل منزلة الإضافة المحضة فإن الشخص الواحد يكون عن يمينك ثم يرجع إلى قدامك ثم إلى شمالك فتتعاقب عليك الإضافات والمتغير ذلك الشخص المنتقل دونك وهكذا ينبغي أن يفهم الحال في علم الله عز وجل فإنا نسلم أنه يعلم الأشياء بعلم واحد في الأزل والأبد والحال لا يتغير وغرضهم نفي التغير وهو متفق عليه
And the objection is from two aspects: One of them is to say: Then you deny those who say that God, the Exalted, has one knowledge of the existence of an eclipse, for example, at a specific time. That knowledge before its existence is knowledge that it will be, and it itself at existence is knowledge that it is occurring, and it itself after the clearing is knowledge of the cessation. And that these differences are due to relations, which do not necessitate a change in the essence of knowledge, so they do not necessitate change in the essence of the knower. For that is like a pure relation. For a single person may be on your right, then he moves to your front, then to your left. The relations change upon you, but the one changing is that moving person, not you. And thus should the situation be understood concerning the knowledge of God, the Mighty and Majestic. For we concede that He knows things with one knowledge, in pre-eternity and eternity and the present, without change. And their purpose is to deny change, and that is agreed upon.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection from the philosophers against the Ash'ari position that God's knowledge changes with the object. The objection uses the example of an eclipse: knowledge before, during, and after is the same knowledge but with different relations. The analogy of a person moving around you illustrates that the knower (you) does not change; only the relation changes. Ghazali concedes that this is valid and agrees that God's knowledge does not change, noting that the purpose of denying change is agreed upon.

How One Knowledge Covers Different Times
Ghazali refutes the claim that one knowledge cannot cover past, present, and future.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that a single knowledge can encompass multiple temporal states and how he turns the philosophers' objection against their own view of God's knowledge.

Core Idea
A single persistent knowledge can cover past, present, and future.

Ghazali now addresses the philosophers' second objection: that knowledge of a contingent changing over time would require change in God's knowledge. He presents a striking counterexample from daily experience—a persistent knowledge of a future event suffices for all its temporal states. Then he turns the argument against the philosophers, showing that their own position on divine knowledge leads to a contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the counterexample
Read Ghazali's example of knowing Zayd's arrival tomorrow. Notice how a single knowledge remains sufficient through different times.
2
Understand the philosophers' objection
Identify the philosophers' claim that different relations require different knowledges.
3
Follow the reductio
See how Ghazali applies this same reasoning to the philosophers' view that God knows all universals with one knowledge.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Understand why Ghazali finds it inconsistent to allow one knowledge for diverse species but not for temporal states of one thing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقولهم : من ضرورة إثبات العلم بالكون الآن والانقضاء بعده تغير فليس بمسلم فمن أين عرفوا ذلك فلو خلق الله تعالى لنا علماً بقدوم زيد غداً عند طلوع الشمس وأدام هذا العلم ولم يخلق لنا علماً آخر لكنا عند طلوع الشمس عالمين بمجرد العلم السابق بقدومه الآن وبعده بأنه قد قدم من قبل وكان ذلك العلم الواحد الباقي كافياً في الإحاطة بهذه الأحوال الثلاثة فيبقى قولهم إن الإضافة إلى المعلوم المعين داخلة في حقيقته ومهما اختلفت الإضافة اختلف الشيء الذي الإضافة ذاتية له ومهما حصل الاختلاف والتعاقب فقد حصل التغير فنقول إن صح هذا فاسلكوا مسلك إخوانكم من الفلاسفة حيث قالوا إنه لا يعلم إلا نفسه وإن علمه بذاته عين ذاته لأنه لو علم الإنسان المطلق والحيوان المطلق والجماد المطلق وهذه مختلفات لا محالة فالإضافات إليها تختلف لا محالة فلا يصلح العلم الواحد لأن يكون علماً بالمختلفات لأن المضاف مختلف والإضافة مختلفة والإضافة إلى المعلوم ذاتية للعلم فيوجب ذلك تعدداً واختلافاً لا تعدداً فقط مع التماثل إذ المتماثلات ما يسد بعضها مسد بعض والعلم بالحيوان لا يسد مسد العلم بالبياض لا يسد مسد العلم بالسواد فهي مختلفات ثم إن هذه الأنواع والأجناس والعوارض الكلية لا نهاية لها وهي مختلفة والعلوم المختلفة كيف تنطوي تحت علم واحد ثم ذلك العلم هو العلم بذات العالم من غير مزيد عليه وليت شعري كيف يستجيز العاقل من نفسه أن يحيل الاتحاد في العلم بالشيء الواحد المنقسمة أحواله إلى الماضي والمستقبل والآن وهو لا يحيل الاتحاد في العلم المتعلق بجميع الأجناس والأنواع المختلفة والاختلاف والتباعد بين الأجناس والأنواع المتباينة أشد من الاختلاف الواقع بين
And their saying: 'From the necessity of affirming knowledge of existence now and cessation after is change' is not conceded. For where do they know that? For if God the Exalted created for us knowledge of the arrival of Zayd tomorrow at sunrise, and perpetuated this knowledge and did not create another knowledge for us, then at sunrise we would know — by that previous knowledge — his arrival now, and after it that he had arrived before, and that one remaining knowledge would be sufficient to encompass these three states. So their saying remains: 'The relation to the particular known is included in its reality, and whenever the relation differs, the thing for which relation is essential differs, and whenever difference and succession occur, change has occurred.' Then we say: If this is correct, then adopt the way of your brethren among the philosophers where they said: He knows only Himself, and His knowledge of His essence is identical to His essence. For if He knew the absolute man and the absolute animal and the absolute inanimate, and these are necessarily diverse, then the relations to them are necessarily diverse. So a single knowledge cannot be knowledge of diverse things, because the related is diverse and the relation is diverse, and the relation to the known is essential to knowledge. That necessitates multiplicity and difference, not merely multiplicity with similarity — for similar things are those that can substitute for each other, and knowledge of animal cannot substitute for knowledge of whiteness cannot substitute for knowledge of blackness. They are diverse. Then these species and genera and universal accidents are infinite and diverse. How can diverse knowledges be subsumed under one knowledge? Then that knowledge is knowledge of the essence of the world without anything added? Would that I knew how a rational person can permit himself to deem impossible unity in knowledge of a single thing whose states are divided into past, future, and present, while he does not deem impossible unity in knowledge related to all diverse genera and species? For the difference and distance between diverse genera and species is more intense than the difference occurring between [the states of a single thing].
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the second objection. Ghazali first rejects the claim that knowledge of existence now and cessation after necessarily implies change in knowledge, offering a counterexample: a persistent knowledge of Zayd's arrival tomorrow suffices to know his arrival now and his past arrival. He then turns the tables on the philosophers: if they insist that different relations require different knowledges, then their own view that God's one knowledge knows all diverse universals (e.g., man, animal, inanimate) is impossible, because the relations to these diverse objects are different and thus would require multiple knowledges. However, they claim God's knowledge is one and identical with His essence. The difference between temporal states of one thing is less than the difference between disparate genera and species, so if they allow one knowledge for diverse universals, they should also allow one knowledge for one thing's different times. Thus their objection is self-defeating.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge of a changing object must itself change with time.
Correction: A single persistent knowledge can encompass different temporal states, as Ghazali's example shows.

Checkpoint
What example does Ghazali give to show that one knowledge can cover multiple temporal states?
A) Knowledge of an eclipse
B) Knowledge of Zayd's arrival tomorrow
C) Knowledge of God's essence
D) Knowledge of a moving person

Key Takeaway
Ghazali shows that a single knowledge can cover different times, and that the philosophers' objection actually undermines their own position.

Refuting the Claim That Knowledge of Temporal Events Necessitates Change
Ghazali refutes the objection that knowing a temporal event requires change in God's knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali refutes the claim that God's knowledge of temporal events would necessitate change in His essence.

Core Idea
A single knowledge can encompass future, present, and past without change.

This passage presents a classic philosophical objection against divine omniscience: if God knows a temporal event as future, then as present, then as past, doesn't His knowledge change? Ghazali rejects this premise and offers a striking counterexample from human experience to show that a single sustained knowledge can cover multiple temporal states, thereby defending divine immutability.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Pay attention to the structure: it begins with the philosophers' objection, then Ghazali's rejection, followed by a counterexample.
Notice the repetition in the Arabic; it might be a scribal error, but the meaning is clear.
2
Identify the objection
The philosophers argue that if God knows something as present now and later as past, that would entail a change in God's knowledge. Identify their premise and why they think it is necessary.
3
Understand Ghazali's rejection
Ghazali says the premise is 'not conceded' (ghair musallam). He then provides a counterexample: a single knowledge of Zayd's arrival tomorrow at sunrise. If that knowledge persists, it suffices to know his arrival as future yesterday, as present at sunrise, and as past afterward.
The example shows that change in the known object does not force change in the knowledge itself.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this analogy defends God's immutability. If a human can have a single knowledge covering multiple temporal states, then it is even more possible for God. This refutes the objection without conceding change in God.
The key is that knowledge can be about temporal events without itself being temporal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقولهم : من ضرورة إثبات العلم بالكون الآن ، والانقضاء بعده ؛ تغير فليس بمسلم ، فمن أين عرفوا ذلك ؟ ! فلو خلق الله تعالى لنا علماً ، بقدوم زيد غداً ، عند طلوع الشمس ، وأدام هذا العلم ، ولم يخلق لنا علماً آخر ، لكنا عند طلوع الشمس ، عالمين – بمجرد العلم السابق - بقدومه الآن ، وبعده بأنه قد قدم من قبل ، وبعده بأنه قد قدم من قبل ، وكان ذلك العلم الواحد الباقى ، كافياً فى الإحاطة بهذه الأحوال الثلاثة
And their statement: 'From the necessity of affirming knowledge of existence now, and its cessation afterwards, [comes] change' is not conceded, for how do they know that? For if God Almighty created for us a knowledge of the arrival of Zayd tomorrow at sunrise, and sustained this knowledge, and did not create for us another knowledge, we would at sunrise, by virtue of the prior knowledge alone, know his arrival now, and after it that he had arrived before, and after it that he had arrived before, and that single remaining knowledge would be sufficient to encompass these three states.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The philosophers object: if God knows something as present now, and then knows it as past later, this change in the object of knowledge would entail a change in the knowledge itself, hence a change in God. Ghazali rejects this premise as not conceded. He then gives a counterexample: suppose God creates in us a single lasting knowledge of Zayd's arrival tomorrow at sunrise. When sunrise comes, we know his arrival now by that same knowledge; after it, we know that it happened. The knowledge itself does not change; it simply covers all three temporal states (future, present, past) by its persistence. Thus change in the known object does not necessitate change in the knower.

The Inconsistency of the Philosophers' Objection About the Unity of Divine Knowledge
Ghazali uses the philosophers' own principles to show their objection is inconsistent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical inconsistency in the philosophers' objection to the unity of divine knowledge, and how Ghazali uses their own principles to refute them.

Core Idea
Difference in objects does not force change in divine knowledge; the philosophers are inconsistent.

In this passage, Ghazali confronts the philosophers' objection that if God's knowledge relates to different objects, it must change. He turns their own reasoning against them, showing that they themselves affirm a single knowledge encompassing vastly different species. This reveals a serious inconsistency in their position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then the English translation. Notice the structure: Ghazali restates the objection, then offers his counterargument.
Pay attention to the key terms: إضافة (relation), ذاتي (essential), تماثل (similarity).
2
Identify the central inconsistency
Ghazali points out that the philosophers accept a single divine knowledge for all species and genera (which are vastly different), but reject it for temporal states of a single thing. Find this contrast in the text.
The inconsistency is that they allow one knowledge for more diverse objects but not for less diverse ones.
3
Examine the analogy
Ghazali argues that difference between species is greater than difference between temporal states. Understand why this analogy strengthens his case: if unity is possible for the greater, it should be possible for the lesser.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion is that a single, eternal knowledge encompassing all is possible without change in God. Consider how this defends the Islamic view of divine omniscience.
This lesson shows the power of dialectical reasoning in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيبقى قولهم : إن الإضافة إلى المعلوم المعين ، داخلة في حقيقتي ، ومهما اختلفت الإضافة ، اختلف الشيء الذى الإضافة ذاتية له ، ومهما حصل الاختلاف والتعاقب ، فقد حصل التغير · فنقول : إن صح هذا ، فاسلكوا مسلك إخوانكم من الفلاسفة حيث قالوا : إنه لا يعلم إلا نفسه ، وإن علمه بذاته عين ذاته ؛ لأنه لو علم الإنسان المطلق والحيوان المطلق ، والجماد المطلق ، وهذه مختلفات لا محالة ، فالإضافات إليها تختلف لا محالة ، فلا يصلح العلم الواحد لأن يكون علماً بالمختلفات ، لأن المضاف مختلف، والإضافة مختلفة ، والإضافة إلى المعلوم ذاتية للعلم ، فيوجب ذلك تعدداً واختلافاً ، لا تعدداً فقط مع التماثل ، إذ المتماثلات ما يسد بعضها مسد بعض ، والعلم بالحيوان لا يسد مسد والعلم بالبياض لا يسد مسد العلم بالسواد ، فهي مختلفات ثم إن هذه الأنواع والأجناس ، والعوارض الكلية ، لا نهاية لها ، وهى مختلفة ، والعلوم المختلفة ، كيف تنطوى تحت علم واحد ؟ ! ثم ذلك العلم هو ذات العالم ، من غير مزيد عليه ولیت شعرى كيف يستجيز العاقل من نفسه ، أن يحيل الاتحاد في العلم بالشيء الواحد ، المنقسمة أحواله : إلى الماضى ، والمستقبل ، والآن ، وهو لا يحيل الاتحاد في العلم المتعلق ، بجميع الأجناس والأنواع المختلفة ، والاختلاف والتباعد بين الأجناس والأنواع المتباينة ، أشد من الاختلاف الواقع ، بين أحوال الشيء الواحد ، المنقسم بانقسام الزمان ؛ وإذا لم يوجب ذلك تعدداً واختلافاً ، فكيف يوجب هذا تعدداً واختلافاً ؟ ! ومهما ثبت بالبرهان ، أن اختلاف الأزمان ، دون اختلاف الأجناس والأنواع ، وأن ذلك لم يوجب التعدد والاختلاف ، فهذا أيضاً لا يوجب الاختلاف وإذا لم يوجب الاختلاف ، جازت الإحاطة بالكل ، بعلم واحد ، دائم في الأزل والأبد ، ولا يوجب ذلك تغيراً في ذات العالم
So remains their statement: 'The relation to the specific known is included in its essence, and whenever the relation differs, the thing for which the relation is essential differs, and whenever difference and succession occur, change has occurred.' So we say: if this is correct, then follow the path of your brethren among the philosophers who said: He does not know except Himself, and His knowledge of His essence is identical to His essence; because if He knew absolute man, absolute animal, and absolute inanimate, and these are necessarily different, then the relations to them would necessarily differ, so one knowledge cannot be knowledge of different things, because the related is different and the relation is different, and the relation to the known is essential to knowledge, so that necessitates multiplicity and difference, not mere multiplicity with similarity, since similar things are those that can substitute for one another, and knowledge of animal does not substitute for knowledge of whiteness, nor does knowledge of whiteness substitute for knowledge of blackness—they are different. Then these species and genera and universal accidents are infinite and diverse; how can diverse knowledges be subsumed under one knowledge? Then that knowledge is the essence of the world, with no addition. Would that I knew how a rational person can allow himself to consider impossible the unity of knowledge concerning a single thing whose states are divided into past, future, and present, while he does not consider impossible the unity of knowledge concerning all the different genera and species—and the difference and distance between disparate genera and species is more intense than the difference occurring between the states of a single thing divided by the division of time. If that does not necessitate multiplicity and difference, how does this necessitate multiplicity and difference? And whenever it is established by proof that the difference of times is inferior to the difference of genera and species, and that that did not necessitate multiplicity and difference, then this also does not necessitate difference. And when it does not necessitate difference, it is permissible to encompass the whole with a single knowledge, eternal in pre-eternity and post-eternity, and that does not necessitate a change in the essence of the world.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage is a rebuttal to the philosophers' argument that if God's knowledge relates to different objects, the relations differ, so the knowledge must change. Ghazali first restates their position: relation to the known is essential to knowledge, so different relations imply different knowledges, and temporal succession implies change. He then turns their own principle against them: if difference in relation necessitates difference in knowledge, then how can the philosophers claim that God knows all species and genera with a single knowledge? For the objects are vastly different (man, animal, inanimate), yet they affirm a single divine knowledge. Moreover, the difference between species is far greater than the difference between temporal states of a single object. If they accept unity of knowledge for diverse species, consistency demands they accept unity for temporal states of one object. Therefore, their objection fails, and a single eternal knowledge encompassing all is possible without change in God.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Difference in relation always forces multiplicity and change in the subject.
Correction: The philosophers themselves affirm a single knowledge for diverse objects (species and genera), so their objection to temporal states is inconsistent; thus difference in relation does not necessarily force change.

Second Objection: Why Can't God's Knowledge Change?
Ghazali presents the second objection and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's strategy in the second objection: how he uses the philosophers' own acceptance of change in an eternal world to challenge their rejection of change in God's knowledge.

Core Idea
Ghazali turns the philosophers' own principle against them.

This passage presents the second major objection against the philosophers' view of God's knowledge. Ghazali begins by asking a simple question: why must God's knowledge be unchanging? He then notes that earlier theologians (Jahm and Karramiyya) actually accepted change in God, and the orthodox rejected them only because change implies origination. But the philosophers themselves believe in an eternal yet changing world, so their objection is inconsistent. Finally, he anticipates their deeper argument about how an originated knowledge could arise in God.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation to get the flow of the argument.
The Arabic has a possible textual corruption, but the meaning is clear.
2
Identify the objection and cited groups
Note the question: why can't God's knowledge change? Then find the two groups (Jahm and Karramiyya) that accepted change in God.
Jahm held that God's knowledge of events is originated; Karramiyya held God is a locus for events.
3
Understand the orthodox reply and ad hominem
The orthodox reply is that change implies origination. Then Ghazali says: but you philosophers accept an eternal world that changes, so you should accept change in God too.
This is a tu quoque argument.
4
Examine the deeper rebuttal
Ghazali anticipates a response: originated knowledge in God must come from Him (impossible, as an eternal cannot become an agent) or from another (impossible, as nothing can affect God).
This reinforces the challenge to the philosophers.
5
Reflect on the inconsistency
Ask yourself: why is the philosophers' position inconsistent? How does Ghazali use their own principles against them?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاعتراض الثانى هو أن يقال : وما المانع على أصلكم من أن يعلم هذه الأمور الجزئية ، وإن كان يتغير ؟ ! وهلا اعتقدتم : أن هذا النوع من التغير ، لا يستحيل عليه ، كما ذهب « جهم » من « المعتزلة » إلى أن علومه بالحوادث حادثة ، وكما اعتقد « الكرامية ) من عند آخرهم ، أنه محل للحوادث ، ولم ينكر جماهير أهل الحق عليهم ، إلا من حيث إن المتغير ، لا يخلو عن التغير ، وما لا يخلو عن التغير والحوادث ، فهو حادث ، وليس بقديم . وأما أنتم فمذهبكم أن العالم قديم ، وأنه لا يخلو عن التغير ، فإذا عقلتم قديماً متغيراً ، فلامانع لكم من هذا الاعتقاد فإن قيل : إنما أحلنا ذلك ، لأن العلم الحادث في ذاته ، لا يخلو : إما أن يحدث من جهته ، أو من جهة غيره ولا باطل أن يحدث منه يحدث منه ، فإنا بينا أن القديم ، لا يصدر منه حادث ، فاعلا ، بعد أن لم يكن فاعلا ، فإنه يوجب تغيراً ، وقد قررناه في يصير مسألة حدوث العالم • و إن حصل ذلك فى ذاته من جهة غيره ، فكيف يكون غيره مؤثراً فيه ومغيراً له ، حتى تتغير أحواله ، على سبيل التسخير والاضطرار من جهة غيره ؟!
The second objection is to say: What prevents, according to your principle, that He knows these particular things, even if it [knowledge] changes? Why do you not believe that this type of change is not impossible for Him, just as Jahm of the Mu'tazila held that His knowledges of events are originated, and as the Karramiyya at their end believed that He is a locus for originated events? The masses of the people of truth did not deny them except on the grounds that that which changes is not free from change, and whatever is not free from change and originated events is originated, not eternal. But you—your doctrine is that the world is eternal and that it is not free from change. So if you conceive of an eternal thing that changes, then there is no impediment for you to this belief. If it is said: We only deemed it impossible because the knowledge that originates in His essence is necessarily either originated from Him or from another. And it is false that it originates from Him, for we have shown that the eternal does not produce an originated thing, becoming an agent after not being an agent, for that necessitates change—and we have established this in the issue of the world's origination. And if it occurs in His essence from another, then how can another be effective upon Him and change Him so that His states change by way of subjugation and compulsion from another?
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection and its answer. The objection: why can God's knowledge not be subject to change? The philosophers assume change in God is impossible, but Ghazali notes that other sects (Jahm ibn Safwan and the Karramiyya) affirmed originated knowledge in God or that God is a locus of events, and the orthodox rejection was based on the argument that whatever changes is originated, not eternal. However, the philosophers themselves hold that the world is eternal yet changing. So if they can accept an eternal changing world, they should have no objection to an eternal changing knowledge. The implied response: their own principle allows change in the eternal. Then Ghazali anticipates a rebuttal: the philosophers might say that an originated knowledge in God would have to come from Him (impossible because an eternal agent cannot become an agent) or from another (impossible because nothing can affect God). Ghazali leaves this as a challenge to them, implying their position is inconsistent.

Correction: Ghazali shows that some theologians (Jahm, Karramiyya) did accept change in God, and the orthodox rejection was based on a specific argument (change implies origination). The philosophers' position is inconsistent because they accept change in an eternal world.
