The Philosophers' Proof: Knowledge Requires an Indivisible Locus
Ghazali presents the philosophers' proof using a conditional syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' conditional syllogism arguing that the soul must be indivisible because knowledge is indivisible.

Core Idea
Knowledge that cannot be divided must inhere in a locus that cannot be divided.

In this passage, Ghazali presents the philosophers' first proof for the soul's indivisibility. They use a conditional syllogism: if the locus of knowledge were a divisible body, knowledge itself would be divisible; but knowledge is not divisible, so its locus cannot be a body. Ghazali affirms the logical validity and the premises.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' argument
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the conditional syllogism structure.
The argument is presented as a qiyas sharti.
2
Identify the two premises
First premise: if the locus is divisible, knowledge is divisible. Second premise: knowledge is indivisible.
The second premise is justified by the fact that we cannot imagine splitting knowledge.
3
Understand the logical form
It is a conditional syllogism affirming the contradictory of the consequent to derive the contradictory of the antecedent.
Ghazali says this form is valid by consensus.
4
Reflect on why knowledge is indivisible
Consider Ghazali's example: we cannot suppose eliminating part of knowledge while the rest remains, because knowledge has no parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قولهم الأول : إن العلوم العقلية ، تحل النفوس الإنسانية ، وهي محصورة ، وفيها آحاد لا تنقسم ، فلابد أن يكون محلها أيضاً لا ينقسم ، وكل جسم ، فمنقسم فدل على أن محلها شيء لا ينقسم . ويمكن إيراد هذا على شرط المنطق بأشكاله ، ولكن أقربه أن يقال : إن كان محل العلم جسما منقسما ، فالعلم الحال فيه أيضاً منقسم ، لكن الحال غير منقسم ، فالمحل ليس جسما . وهذا « قياس شرطى ( استثنى فيه « نقيض التالي » ، فينتج » نقيض المقدم ( بالاتفاق ، فلا نظر فى صحة شكل القياس ، ولا نظر أيضاً في المقدمتين ، فإن : الأول ، قولنا : إن كل حال في منقسم ، ينقسم لا محالة بفرض القسمة في محله ، وهو أولى لا يمكن التشكك فيه . والثانى ، قولنا : إن العلم الواحد يحل في الآدمى ، وهو لا ينقسم ، لأنه لو انقسم ، إلى غير نهاية ، كان محالا ، وإن كانت له نهاية ، فيشتمل على آحاد لا محالة لا تنقسم ، وعلى الجملة نحن نعلم أشياء ، ولا نقدر أن نفرض زوال بعضها ، وبقاء البعض ، من حيث إنه لا بعض لها . والاعتراض على مقامين : المقام الأول : أن يقال ، بم تنكرون على من يقول : محل العلم جوهر فرد متحيز لا ينقسم ، وقد عرف هذا من مذهب المتكلمين . ولا يبقى بعده إلا الاستبعاد ، وهو أنه كيف تحل العلوم كلها ، في جوهر فرد ، وتكون جميع الجواهر المطيفة بها ، معطلة وهى مجاورة ؟ ! والاستبعاد لا خير فيه ، إذ يتوجه على مذهبهم أيضاً ، أنه كيف تكون النفس شيئاً واحداً لا يتحيز ، ولا يشار إليه ، ولا يكون داخل البدن ، ولا خارجه ، ولا متصلا بالجسم ولا منفصلا عنه ؟ ! إلا أننا ، لا نؤثر هذا المقام ، فإن القول في مسألة الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ طويل ، ولهم فيه أدلة هندسية يطول الكلام عليها ، ومن جملتها قولهم : جوهر فرد ، بین جوهرین ، هل يلاقى أحد الطرفين منه عين ما يلاقيه الآخر ؟ ! أو غيره ؟ ! فإن كان عينه فهو محال ، إذ يلزم منه تلاقى الطرفين ، فإن ملاقي الملاقي ملاق ، وإن كان ما يلاقيه غيره ، ففيه إثبات التعدد والانقسام ، وهذه شبهة يطول حلها (۱) ، وبنا غنية عن الخوض فيها ، فلنعدل إلى مقام آخر
Their first statement: The rational sciences inhere in human souls, and they are finite, and among them are indivisible units. Therefore, their locus must also be indivisible. And every body is divisible. So this indicates that their locus is something not divisible. This can be presented in the form of a conditional syllogism with its figures, but the closest way is to say: If the locus of knowledge were a divisible body, then the knowledge inhering in it would also be divisible. But the inhering thing (knowledge) is not divisible. Therefore, the locus is not a body. This is a conditional syllogism in which the contradictory of the consequent is affirmed, producing the contradictory of the antecedent unanimously, so there is no need to examine the validity of the syllogism's form, nor the two premises. The first premise: our statement that everything inhering in something divisible becomes divisible necessarily by assuming division in its locus, and this is obvious and cannot be doubted. The second premise: our statement that a single knowledge inheres in a human and it is not divisible, because if it were divisible indefinitely it would be impossible, and if it had a limit, then it would necessarily contain indivisible units. In summary, we know things and we cannot suppose the elimination of some of them while the rest remain, because they have no parts. The objection is from two positions: The first position: it could be said, why do you reject the view of those who say that the locus of knowledge is an individual atom (jawhar fard) that occupies space and is indivisible? This is known from the doctrine of the mutakallimun. Nothing remains after that except to find it improbable: how can all sciences inhere in a single atom while all the surrounding atoms are devoid [of knowledge] despite being adjacent? But improbability is no argument, for the same objection can be made against their own view: how can the soul be a single thing that does not occupy space, cannot be pointed to, is neither inside the body nor outside, neither connected to the body nor separate from it? However, we do not prefer this position, for the discussion of the indivisible atom is lengthy and they have geometric arguments about which much could be said. Among them is their statement: an individual atom between two atoms—does one of its sides meet exactly what the other side meets, or something else? If it is the same, it is impossible because it would necessitate meeting of the two sides, for whatever meets what meets is a meeting. If what it meets is different, that entails multiplicity and division. This is a specious argument whose resolution is lengthy. We are independent of delving into it. So let us turn to another position.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' first proof for the soul's indivisibility and Ghazali's first objection, which he then dismisses. The philosophers argue that rational knowledge consists of indivisible units (since knowledge is not divisible into parts like bodies), so their locus (the soul) must also be indivisible. They formalize this as a conditional syllogism: if the locus (e.g., a body) were divisible, the knowledge inhering in it would be divisible; but knowledge is not divisible, so the locus is not a body. Ghazali endorses the logical form and premises, calling the first premise obvious and the second premise justified by the fact that we cannot conceive of some parts of knowledge remaining while others cease, because knowledge is a unity without parts. Then Ghazali presents his first objection: a critic might say the locus of knowledge could be an indivisible atom (jawhar fard), a position held by the mutakallimun. The objection would then accuse the philosophers of merely finding it improbable that all knowledge inheres in a single atom while surrounding atoms lack it. But Ghazali notes that improbability is not a decisive argument; the philosophers' own view that the soul is non-spatial and not inside or outside the body is equally 'improbable'. However, Ghazali chooses not to pursue this line because the debate over the indivisible atom is long and involves geometric arguments (such as the puzzle of what an atom's sides meet). He concludes by setting aside this first objection and moving to a different approach.

Key Terms
محل (locus)
علم (knowledge)
قياس شرطى (conditional syllogism)
منقسم (divisible)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What logical form does the philosophers' proof take?
A) Categorical syllogism
B) Conditional syllogism
C) Disjunctive syllogism
D) Hypothetical syllogism

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that since knowledge is indivisible, its locus cannot be a divisible body; thus the soul must be indivisible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your knowledge is indivisible and resides in an indivisible soul, what does that say about the permanence of your identity as a knower? How does that understanding change the way you treat your own mind and soul?
?
The philosophers argue that a single unit of knowledge cannot be divided. Consider a time when you were certain of a truth. Did that certainty have parts? How does the indivisibility of such knowledge challenge the way you handle doubts or distractions?
?
Since the soul is not a body, what does that mean for your attachment to physical things? Are you more invested in your physical body or in the knowledge that defines your soul? What would it take to shift that balance?

Divine Attributes

Ghazali's Objection: The Indivisible Atom and Why He Moves On
Ghazali raises the atomist objection and explains why he sets it aside.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's first objection to the philosophers' proof—that the locus could be an indivisible atom—and why he chooses not to pursue this line of argument.

Core Idea
The objection from the indivisible atom is plausible but leads to a lengthy debate Ghazali prefers to avoid.

After presenting the philosophers' proof, Ghazali raises an objection from the position of the mutakallimun: why can't the locus of knowledge be an indivisible atom? He explains the objection and notes that it can be countered with accusations of improbability, but such accusations also apply to the philosophers' own view of the soul. However, Ghazali decides not to pursue this atomic debate because it involves intricate geometric arguments that would prolong the discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objection: the locus could be a jawhar fard (individual atom) as the mutakallimun hold.
This is a direct challenge to the philosophers' conclusion.
2
Understand Ghazali's critique of the objection
Ghazali says that calling it improbable is not a strong argument, because the philosophers' own view of the soul is equally improbable.
3
See why Ghazali drops this objection
He states that the debate on the indivisible atom is lengthy and involves geometric puzzles, so he will move to another position.
He avoids getting sidetracked.
4
Reflect on the geometric puzzle
The puzzle of an atom between two atoms and what its sides meet illustrates the complexity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قولهم الأول : إن العلوم العقلية ، تحل النفوس الإنسانية ، وهي محصورة ، وفيها آحاد لا تنقسم ، فلابد أن يكون محلها أيضاً لا ينقسم ، وكل جسم ، فمنقسم فدل على أن محلها شيء لا ينقسم . ويمكن إيراد هذا على شرط المنطق بأشكاله ، ولكن أقربه أن يقال : إن كان محل العلم جسما منقسما ، فالعلم الحال فيه أيضاً منقسم ، لكن الحال غير منقسم ، فالمحل ليس جسما . وهذا « قياس شرطى ( استثنى فيه « نقيض التالي » ، فينتج » نقيض المقدم ( بالاتفاق ، فلا نظر فى صحة شكل القياس ، ولا نظر أيضاً في المقدمتين ، فإن : الأول ، قولنا : إن كل حال في منقسم ، ينقسم لا محالة بفرض القسمة في محله ، وهو أولى لا يمكن التشكك فيه . والثانى ، قولنا : إن العلم الواحد يحل في الآدمى ، وهو لا ينقسم ، لأنه لو انقسم ، إلى غير نهاية ، كان محالا ، وإن كانت له نهاية ، فيشتمل على آحاد لا محالة لا تنقسم ، وعلى الجملة نحن نعلم أشياء ، ولا نقدر أن نفرض زوال بعضها ، وبقاء البعض ، من حيث إنه لا بعض لها . والاعتراض على مقامين : المقام الأول : أن يقال ، بم تنكرون على من يقول : محل العلم جوهر فرد متحيز لا ينقسم ، وقد عرف هذا من مذهب المتكلمين . ولا يبقى بعده إلا الاستبعاد ، وهو أنه كيف تحل العلوم كلها ، في جوهر فرد ، وتكون جميع الجواهر المطيفة بها ، معطلة وهى مجاورة ؟ ! والاستبعاد لا خير فيه ، إذ يتوجه على مذهبهم أيضاً ، أنه كيف تكون النفس شيئاً واحداً لا يتحيز ، ولا يشار إليه ، ولا يكون داخل البدن ، ولا خارجه ، ولا متصلا بالجسم ولا منفصلا عنه ؟ ! إلا أننا ، لا نؤثر هذا المقام ، فإن القول في مسألة الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ طويل ، ولهم فيه أدلة هندسية يطول الكلام عليها ، ومن جملتها قولهم : جوهر فرد ، بین جوهرین ، هل يلاقى أحد الطرفين منه عين ما يلاقيه الآخر ؟ ! أو غيره ؟ ! فإن كان عينه فهو محال ، إذ يلزم منه تلاقى الطرفين ، فإن ملاقي الملاقي ملاق ، وإن كان ما يلاقيه غيره ، ففيه إثبات التعدد والانقسام ، وهذه شبهة يطول حلها (۱) ، وبنا غنية عن الخوض فيها ، فلنعدل إلى مقام آخر
Their first statement: The rational sciences inhere in human souls, and they are finite, and among them are indivisible units. Therefore, their locus must also be indivisible. And every body is divisible. So this indicates that their locus is something not divisible. This can be presented in the form of a conditional syllogism with its figures, but the closest way is to say: If the locus of knowledge were a divisible body, then the knowledge inhering in it would also be divisible. But the inhering thing (knowledge) is not divisible. Therefore, the locus is not a body. This is a conditional syllogism in which the contradictory of the consequent is affirmed, producing the contradictory of the antecedent unanimously, so there is no need to examine the validity of the syllogism's form, nor the two premises. The first premise: our statement that everything inhering in something divisible becomes divisible necessarily by assuming division in its locus, and this is obvious and cannot be doubted. The second premise: our statement that a single knowledge inheres in a human and it is not divisible, because if it were divisible indefinitely it would be impossible, and if it had a limit, then it would necessarily contain indivisible units. In summary, we know things and we cannot suppose the elimination of some of them while the rest remain, because they have no parts. The objection is from two positions: The first position: it could be said, why do you reject the view of those who say that the locus of knowledge is an individual atom (jawhar fard) that occupies space and is indivisible? This is known from the doctrine of the mutakallimun. Nothing remains after that except to find it improbable: how can all sciences inhere in a single atom while all the surrounding atoms are devoid [of knowledge] despite being adjacent? But improbability is no argument, for the same objection can be made against their own view: how can the soul be a single thing that does not occupy space, cannot be pointed to, is neither inside the body nor outside, neither connected to the body nor separate from it? However, we do not prefer this position, for the discussion of the indivisible atom is lengthy and they have geometric arguments about which much could be said. Among them is their statement: an individual atom between two atoms—does one of its sides meet exactly what the other side meets, or something else? If it is the same, it is impossible because it would necessitate meeting of the two sides, for whatever meets what meets is a meeting. If what it meets is different, that entails multiplicity and division. This is a specious argument whose resolution is lengthy. We are independent of delving into it. So let us turn to another position.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' first proof for the soul's indivisibility and Ghazali's first objection, which he then dismisses. The philosophers argue that rational knowledge consists of indivisible units (since knowledge is not divisible into parts like bodies), so their locus (the soul) must also be indivisible. They formalize this as a conditional syllogism: if the locus (e.g., a body) were divisible, the knowledge inhering in it would be divisible; but knowledge is not divisible, so the locus is not a body. Ghazali endorses the logical form and premises, calling the first premise obvious and the second premise justified by the fact that we cannot conceive of some parts of knowledge remaining while others cease, because knowledge is a unity without parts. Then Ghazali presents his first objection: a critic might say the locus of knowledge could be an indivisible atom (jawhar fard), a position held by the mutakallimun. The objection would then accuse the philosophers of merely finding it improbable that all knowledge inheres in a single atom while surrounding atoms lack it. But Ghazali notes that improbability is not a decisive argument; the philosophers' own view that the soul is non-spatial and not inside or outside the body is equally 'improbable'. However, Ghazali chooses not to pursue this line because the debate over the indivisible atom is long and involves geometric arguments (such as the puzzle of what an atom's sides meet). He concludes by setting aside this first objection and moving to a different approach.

Key Terms
جوهر فرد (individual atom)
متحيز (occupying space)
استبعاد (improbability)
الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ (indivisible atom)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali fully endorses the atomist objection.
Correction: He only uses it to show a weakness and then sets it aside because it leads to lengthy debates.

Checkpoint
Why does Ghazali decide not to pursue the objection based on the indivisible atom?
A) Because the objection is logically flawed
B) Because the debate over the atom is lengthy and he prefers a different approach
C) Because the philosophers proved him wrong
D) Because the mutakallimun recanted their view

Key Takeaway
Ghazali uses the atomist position to challenge the philosophers but then avoids the complex atomic debate, shifting to a different argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a truth claim simply because it seemed improbable, without examining whether your own worldview contains equally improbable elements? In what area of your faith or beliefs are you doing that right now? What would it take to admit that your own position might face the same difficulty?
?
Ghazali sets aside a lengthy debate to focus on what he considers more productive. Is there a theological or personal issue you have been avoiding because it's complex or uncomfortable? What would it mean to turn toward it today, even if resolution is not immediate?
?
The philosophers use geometric puzzles to undermine the atomist view, but Ghazali finds them specious and chooses not to engage. Are there arguments or criticisms you've encountered that seem clever but you sense are misleading? How do you discern when to engage and when to move on to more solid ground?
